## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 08:46 UTC

I've read all four docs (`spec.md`, `physics_spec.md`, `fracture_spec.md`/`modules.md`) and cross-checked them against the actual code in [PolygonUtils.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/PolygonUtils.cs), [CompoundShape.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs), [CollisionSystem.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs), and the fracture handler in [Program.cs](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs). Here's my honest assessment.

## First, the good news — this is a strong codebase

Don't let the critique below obscure this: the engineering quality is high and several things are genuinely well done.

- **ECS + sparse sets, Engine/Game separation, config externalization** — all the right calls, cleanly executed.
- **The energy model is better than most shipped arcade games.** Reduced-mass impact energy, spin pre-stress (`E_spin`), sub-threshold accumulation in `FractureState.AbsorbedEnergy` — that's real thought, not hand-waving.
- **The rotational collision impulse is correctly implemented** ([CollisionSystem.cs:128](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L128)) — full Baraff-Witkin form with the `(r×n)²/I` terms. Your own physics_spec still lists this as "⏳ Planned," so the code is ahead of the docs here.
- **The centroid invariant discipline** (local verts centroid-relative, `Transform.Position` = world centroid) is maintained carefully everywhere. That's the kind of thing that silently corrupts a physics engine, and you've stayed on top of it.

Now the substance.

## The core concern: your fracture model is a geometric heuristic, not "physics-based"

Your stated goal is *"realistic and complex physics-based destruction."* But what `PolygonUtils.Split` actually does is a **procedural recipe**: one perpendicular cut, a fan of radial cone cuts, then inner cuts through the impact point. The *gating* is energy-driven (good), but the *crack geometry itself* is a hand-tuned arrangement of half-plane clips. It's "physics-flavored," not physics-derived.

That's not automatically wrong — but it's worth being honest that this specific scheme is **more complex and less robust than the industry-standard approach**, while producing arguably less natural results. Your own TODO list is the tell:

> - "See if the remaining far-side pieces always create convex shapes."
> - "Blast zone logic... fractures are almost never eligible to be discarded."
> - "Sometimes compound shapes aren't connected."

These aren't small bugs. The third one is **fundamental**: your radial petals + primary far-piece are each convex, but nothing guarantees their *union is connected*. A petal can be a geometric island floating away from the main body, and your model has no way to know — because it never asks the connectivity question. That's a structural limitation of the cut-fan approach, not a tuning issue.

## The standard best practice for 2D runtime destruction

For brittle shatter, the proven approach (Box2D fracture demos, most shipped 2D games) is **impact-clustered Voronoi**:

1. Scatter N seed points inside the polygon, **density falling off with distance from the impact** (many near the hit, few far away).
2. Build the Voronoi diagram, clip each cell to the parent polygon (your `ClipConvexByHalfPlane` is exactly the primitive needed — a Voronoi cell *is* the intersection of half-planes).
3. Each clipped cell becomes a fragment.

This gives you the "small pieces near impact, big chunks far away" look **automatically from the seed distribution**, every cell is convex by construction, it handles a concave parent via clipping, and N scales cleanly with energy. It replaces your three-phase zone/cone/petal machinery with one well-understood algorithm — and it's *less* code than what you have now.

## But your actual vision points at a different (better) model

Read your own [meta_notes.md:74](info/meta_notes.md#L74):

> *"asteroids composed of simple shapes stuck together; when hit, some pieces destroyed while the rest splits in smaller composed pieces based on the place and force of the impact."*

That is **not** runtime polygon splitting. That's a **pre-fractured bond-graph model**, and it's the honest answer to "realistic, place-and-force-dependent breakup." Your memory file records that you chose "Option 2 (runtime splitting)" over the cellular model — but the cellular model is what your description actually wants. The tension between the two is, I think, the root of the friction you're feeling.

The bond-graph model:

1. **At spawn**, pre-fracture each asteroid once into convex cells (Voronoi). Store them as a `CompoundShape` *plus an adjacency graph* with a bond strength per shared edge (∝ edge length × material toughness).
2. **On impact**, deposit energy into the nearest cell and let it propagate through the graph, attenuating with distance. Break any bond whose accumulated stress exceeds its strength.
3. **Run connected-components** on the surviving bond graph. Each connected component that's now separated from the main body spawns as its own rigid body (mass/inertia = sum of its cells via parallel-axis — which you already compute correctly).

This is what makes breakup depend on *where* and *how hard* you hit, gives you "pieces stuck together that come apart," and — critically — **"connectivity" stops being a bug and becomes the core mechanic.** It also subsumes your compound-shape-fracture gap below for free.

## Concrete limitations & one real bug in the current code

1. **Fracturing a CompoundShape is a no-op.** [Program.cs:179](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs#L179): `if (col.Shape is not PolygonShape poly) return;`. Once an asteroid fractures once and becomes compound, shooting it again does *nothing*. physics_spec §10.4 describes the intended `LastHitPartIndex` → `Split` part → `WithoutPart` → rebuild path, but it isn't wired up. This is your TODO #4 and it's a major gameplay hole. (In a bond-graph model this problem disappears entirely — there's only ever one mechanism.)

2. **Bullet tunnelling is patched, not solved.** `NearestPointOnBoundary` exists *because* fast bullets tunnel under variable timestep. The standard fix: **raycast your bullets** — treat each as a segment from last to current position, do a ray-vs-polygon query, no physics body at all. Cheaper, no tunnelling, and gives you an exact impact point and surface normal for free. For a bullet-heavy game this is the single highest-value change.

3. **Variable timestep + a fracture/impulse sim is a latent instability source.** spec §3.11 defers fixed timestep as "future." For arcade Asteroids that's been fine — but the moment you have stacking fragments and contact resolution, variable `dt` will bite. I'd promote fixed timestep from "future extension" to "now."

4. **CompoundShape collision is O(parts) with no internal broad-phase.** As asteroids become compounds-of-many-parts, every narrow-phase test fans across all parts ([CompoundShape.cs:50](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs#L50)). *This* is your real future perf cliff — not the things §5 of the spec worries about.

## The clearest case of effort in the wrong place

Section 5 of [spec.md](docs/spec.md) — the 7-model parallelism taxonomy, `ISystemMetadata` (explicitly "advisory, not enforced"), the wave scheduler, double-buffered component stores — is a large amount of speculative architecture for a game that peaks at **300–500 entities**. At that scale single-threaded is *completely* fine; you will not measure a difference. The spec even predicts the collision narrow phase is the bottleneck — but at 500 entities it won't be, and point (4) above (compound part-count blowup) is the thing that *will* hurt, and §5 doesn't address it.

This is documentation/design debt: it reads as thorough, but it's planning for a scale problem you don't have while the actual scale problem (compound part counts) goes unplanned. I'd cut or freeze all of §5 and spend that design energy on the fracture model and bullet raycasting.

## What I'd do, in order

1. **Raycast bullets.** Kills tunnelling, gives exact impact point + normal, removes the `NearestPointOnBoundary` patch.
2. **Pick one destruction model and commit.** My recommendation, given your stated vision: **pre-fractured Voronoi cells + bond graph + connected-components**. If you want to stay closer to current code, at minimum replace the cut-fan in `Split` with impact-clustered Voronoi and add a connectivity check that splits disconnected unions into separate entities.
3. **Make fracture-of-compound work** (or make it irrelevant by going bond-graph).
4. **Fixed timestep** before you lean on contact stacking.
5. **Freeze §5.** Revisit only if a profiler ever says so.

Want me to prototype one of these — say, the impact-clustered Voronoi shatter as a drop-in replacement for `Split`, or a sketch of the bond-graph cell model — so you can compare it against what you have before committing? I could also write this review up into your docs as a design-decision record if you'd like it preserved.

## 🧑 User · 2026-05-31 10:22 UTC

What about brittleness property of the materials? If the asteroids are prefragmented once on creation, it means that when an asteroid fractures, its cells is a subset of its parent. What about the fracturing of the cells themselves? How big would the cells be? I want to understand this model more before committing to it

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 10:23 UTC

Great questions — these are exactly the ones that decide whether the model is worth it. You've already spotted the central weakness, so let me be honest about it and then show how the model resolves it.

## You're right: pure pre-fracture has a granularity floor

If you pre-fracture once at spawn into atomic cells, then every fragment's cells are a strict subset of the parent's, forever. Total cell count only ever *decreases* — cells get distributed among separating components and dust. The smallest physics fragment you can ever produce is **one cell**. That's the floor, and it's the real cost of the model.

Whether that's a problem depends entirely on one thing: **is the floor below the threshold where it becomes visually objectionable?** In practice the floor is "dust particles" (visual-only, no collision), and dust looks fine no matter how it's generated. So for most games the floor is a non-issue. But your instinct is correct — if you want a single cell to keep shattering, atomic cells alone won't do it. That's what the next part is for.

## "Fracturing the cells themselves" — three options

**Option A — Atomic cells.** Cells never break; destruction is purely bond-breaking + connected-component separation. Cheapest, simplest, connectivity is the whole mechanic. Floor = cell size. Sub-cell debris is faked with particles.

**Option B — Hierarchical pre-fracture.** Each cell has its *own* finer Voronoi pre-computed (a quadtree of fractures). A cell that takes enough localized energy shatters into its pre-baked sub-cells, which join the bond graph. No runtime geometry, natural LOD — but more memory and bookkeeping, and you still have a (deeper) floor.

**Option C — Atomic cells + runtime re-split of a struck cell (my recommendation).** Cells are atomic *until* a hit deposits more local energy into a single cell than that cell's own toughness. Only then do you runtime-split *that one cell* — using exactly the Voronoi/`Split` code you already have — into sub-cells and splice them into the graph.

Option C is the answer to your question. The common case (most hits) is cheap bond-breaking with zero runtime geometry. The rare case (a hard, concentrated hit on one cell) pays for a single small Voronoi split and gives you unbounded refinement. You get no hard floor where it matters, and you don't pay for it on every shot.

The one fiddly part of C, so you know going in: when a cell splits into sub-cells, you have to reconnect bonds — sub-cells bond to each other, and each sub-cell inherits a bond to whichever of the original cell's neighbors it still geometrically touches. That edge-reattachment is approximate. It's the only genuinely awkward bit of the whole model.

## Where brittleness lives — and why it's *better* here

This is the strongest argument for the model. In your current `Split`, brittleness only nudges cut counts ([physics_spec §5.3](docs/physics_spec.md)). In a bond graph, brittleness controls the **character** of the break, which is the actual visible difference between glass and metal:

The key lever is **how far stress propagates through the graph before it dies.** Deposit the hit's energy into the nearest cell, then propagate it outward bond-by-bond, attenuating each hop:

```
energy reaching neighbor = energy_here × transmission
transmission = lerp(ductileLoss, brittleLoss, brittleness)   // per bond hop
a bond breaks if stress across it > bondStrength (≈ toughness × edge length)
```

- **Glass (brittleness ≈ 1):** transmission ≈ 0.9 → the crack travels across the *whole body* → most bonds break → it shatters into many cells from a single hit.
- **Metal (brittleness ≈ 0.15):** transmission ≈ 0.2 → energy dies within one or two hops → only the struck cell and its immediate neighbors detach → it *chips* and dents instead of shattering.

So brittleness becomes three coordinated knobs, all physically meaningful:
1. **Propagation attenuation** (the star) — shatter-many vs chip-one.
2. **Crack directionality** — a brittle break can pass residual energy preferentially to the bond *in line* with the incoming crack (straight, branching cracks); ductile spreads it diffusely.
3. **Cell-shatter threshold** (Option C) — brittle cells shatter into sub-cells; ductile cells just detach whole.

Note that **bond strength is really *toughness*, not brittleness** — keep them separate, exactly as your `FractureProperties` already does. Toughness = how much energy to break a bond. Brittleness = how the energy spreads. A material can be tough *and* brittle (hard to start a crack, but once started it runs — like thick glass).

## How big should cells be?

Don't pick a global cell count. Pick a **constant cell size per material** — i.e. treat cell size as the material's **grain**:

- Glass → fine grain → small cells → shatters into many shards.
- Rock → coarse grain → big cells → breaks into a few chunks.

Then cell *count* falls out of `asteroidArea / cellArea`, so big asteroids naturally get more cells and small ones fewer — which is also physically correct (constant grain size). No special-casing per asteroid.

Concrete numbers to anchor it, using your existing constants (`MinFragmentArea = 180 px²`):

| Asteroid | Radius | Area | Cell area (rock) | Cells |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Large | 120px | ~45,000 px² | ~1,500 px² (~22px across) | ~30 |
| Medium | 70px | ~15,000 px² | ~1,500 px² | ~10 |
| Small | 40px | ~5,000 px² | ~1,500 px² | ~3–4 |

Three constraints set the cell-area target:
- **Floor:** keep cell area comfortably above `MinFragmentArea` (here ~8× it) so a cell has headroom to itself shatter into sub-shards in Option C before hitting dust.
- **Readability:** a cell wants to be ≥ ~12–15px across to read on screen. ~22px is comfortable.
- **Performance:** this is your real budget. Compound collision is O(parts) per candidate pair ([CompoundShape.cs:50](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs#L50)) with no internal broad-phase. Keep the largest live compound around **≤ 30 parts** and ~30 asteroids on screen and you're fine. That ~30-cell large asteroid is right at the sane ceiling — which is another reason constant-grain works: it stops big asteroids from exploding your part count.

So the honest answer to "how big" is: **a cell is a few hundred to ~1,500 px², chosen per-material as a grain size, tuned so a large asteroid lands around 25–30 cells.**

## One more honest note — you may not even need bonds

The bond graph is what buys you *emergent structural breakup*: "I shot the thin neck and the two lobes separated." If you want that, you need the connectivity graph — that's the whole point. But if all you actually want is "a chunk falls off where I hit, sized by force," you can skip bonds entirely: one rigid body, and a hit detaches the cells within an energy-scaled radius, with an occasional connected-component check. That's much simpler and 80% of the visual payoff. Worth deciding which you're really after before committing — the graph is the expensive 20%.

---

I could build a small standalone harness — generate a Voronoi-celled asteroid, render the cells and bonds, and let you click to deposit energy and watch propagation + separation — without touching the engine. That'd let you feel the brittleness propagation and cell-size tradeoffs directly before you commit a single line of engine code.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 10:34 UTC

Syntax checks clean. The prototype is at [prototypes/fracture-cells.html](prototypes/fracture-cells.html) — it's a single self-contained file, no build, no engine dependency.

## How to run it

Just open the file in a browser:

```bash
xdg-open /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/prototypes/fracture-cells.html
```

Click anywhere on the asteroid to fire a shot; **Reset hit** to refire the same asteroid; **New asteroid** to regenerate geometry.

## What it implements (all the levers we discussed)

- **Voronoi cells from a grain size** — `makeAsteroid()` scatters jittered-grid seeds (constant grain → big asteroids get more cells) and builds each cell by half-plane clipping (`clipHalf`, your `ClipConvexByHalfPlane`).
- **Bond graph** — `rebuildBonds()` bonds two cells when they share a Voronoi edge; strength ∝ shared-edge length × toughness.
- **Crack propagation** — `propagate()` floods energy from the struck cell in descending-energy order; a bond snaps when crossing energy exceeds its strength, then transmits `(e − strength) × transmission` onward. **Brittleness *is* that transmission factor** (`lerp(0.18, 0.96, brittleness)`).
- **Connected components → fragments** — `buildFragments()` runs union-find over surviving bonds; the largest chunk stays as the asteroid, the rest fly apart (lighter = faster), tiny singletons fade as dust.
- **Option C cell re-split** — if a struck cell absorbs more energy than all its bonds combined, `resplitCell()` shatters it into sub-cells and splices them into the graph (unbounded refinement, no granularity floor).

The energy-heat overlay (on by default) tints cells by the crack energy that reached them — so you can literally *see* how far the crack travels.

## Experiments that map to the design questions you asked

| Set this | And you'll see | Which answers |
|---|---|---|
| **Brittleness 1.0**, energy high | Crack reaches the whole body → shatters into many fragments | glass behaviour |
| **Brittleness 0.1** | Energy dies in 1–2 hops → one chip falls off where you hit | metal behaviour — *this is where brittleness lives* |
| **Grain 600 vs 4000** | Many tiny cells vs few chunky ones | "how big should cells be" + the granularity floor |
| **Toughness high** | Cracks stop short even at high energy | toughness ≠ brittleness |
| **Re-split ON**, hit one cell hard | The single struck cell itself shatters finer | "what about fracturing the cells themselves" (Option C) |
| Hit the **thin neck** of an elongated asteroid | The two lobes separate as distinct fragments | emergent place-dependent breakup = why the bond graph earns its keep |

Watch the **fragments / dust** counters in the stats box as you vary brittleness at fixed energy — that number going from 1 (chip) to 15+ (shatter) purely from the brittleness slider is the whole thesis of the model in one knob.

Two honest caveats about the prototype (not the model): the asteroid outline is kept **convex** so cell-clipping stays trivial — a real concave asteroid needs cells clipped against a concave boundary. And separation velocities here are cosmetic (tuned to look right), not derived from the impulse math your engine already has. Both are deliberate simplifications to keep the harness focused on the cell/bond/propagation behaviour, which is the part you wanted to feel before committing.

Once you've played with it: do you want me to (a) write this up as a design-decision record in `docs/`, or (b) start sketching the C# port — the `CellBody` component + bond graph + a `FractureSystem` that replaces the current `Split` path? I'd hold off on either until the prototype has convinced you the model feels right.

## 🧑 User · 2026-05-31 13:10 UTC

This is definitely the right direction. I'm guessing with this model you can model very different shapes and formations by changing toughness and brittleness. I'm wondering about two main things:
1. How does rotation come into play? Is the energy distribution along the edges biased in some way depending on the direction of the hit and the prior rotation of asteroid?
2. How do concave shapes behave with this model?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:12 UTC

Yes — and that's the deeper payoff: toughness/brittleness/grain are three axes, and the two things you're asking about are effectively **two more axes** (rotation pre-stress and impact anisotropy) that fall out of the same bond-energy machinery without bolting on special cases. Let me take them in turn, because the answers are genuinely different in character.

## 1. Rotation

Right now the prototype's propagation is **isotropic** — energy floods outward equally, attenuated only by `transmission`. That's deliberately the simplest thing, and it already gives glass-vs-metal. But you're correct that real fracture is directional, and there are *two separate* rotational/directional effects, which it's worth keeping distinct:

### (A) Impact directionality — the crack has a grain set by the shot

A point impact doesn't load the material evenly. The compressive shock drives *into* the body along the impact direction, and the classic brittle failure is a **Hertzian cone crack** — a cone of damage that penetrates along the shot and spalls a chunk off. The clean way to model this is to make propagation **anisotropic**: weight the transmitted energy by the angle between the crack's direction and the impact direction.

```
// when the crack flows from cell i to neighbour j:
d     = normalize(centroid_j - centroid_i)      // propagation direction
align = dot(d, impactDir)                        // +1 = driving into body, -1 = back toward entry face
gain  = lerp(1, max(0, align)^p, directionality) // directionality 0 = isotropic, 1 = tight forward cone
transmitted = (e - strength) * baseTransmission * gain
```

At `directionality = 0` you get today's behaviour. Crank it up and damage drives forward into the body as a cone/wedge — which reads *much* better than an even splash, because the player sees the shot "punch through."

### (B) Rotational pre-stress — spin primes the body to cleave into wedges

This is the one you're really asking about, and it's the more interesting effect. A spinning body is under **centrifugal tension** — every cell is being flung outward, which builds up **hoop (circumferential) stress**. The failure mode of an over-spun grindstone or flywheel is textbook: it bursts into **radial pie-wedges**, because hoop stress is what opens *radial* cracks.

In bond terms: a radial crack severs the bonds it crosses, and those are the **tangentially-oriented** ones. So spin doesn't add energy uniformly — it *pre-weakens tangential bonds*, biased toward the interior:

```
// per bond, given the body's angular velocity ω:
m   = 0.5*(centroid_i + centroid_j) - CoM        // bond midpoint, relative to centre of mass
rad = normalize(m);  tan = perp(rad)
tangentiality = |dot(normalize(centroid_j - centroid_i), tan)|   // 1 = circumferential bond
preStress     = Kspin * ω^2 * radialProfile(|m|) * tangentiality
effStrength   = max(eps, strength - preStress)   // propagate against this, not the raw strength
```

(`radialProfile` technically peaks at the centre for a solid disk — that's why grindstones burst from the middle — but `∝ |m|` reads fine for a game.)

The consequences are exactly what you'd want emergently:
- A **fast-spinning asteroid needs less bullet energy** to shatter (it's already pre-loaded).
- It tends to **split along radial lines into wedges** rather than chip — even from a hit that would only dent a static one.
- The hit direction and the spin **interact**: a shot that lands where the spin pre-stress is already high triggers a much bigger break.

Note this *subsumes* two hacks in your current spec: the scalar `E_spin = SpinEnergyFraction·½Iω²` bonus and the bolt-on `spinFaultAngle` cut bias both become unnecessary — you instead *distribute* spin energy across the bond graph as a pre-stress field, and the wedge pattern emerges from connectivity. (Separately, fragments still inherit tangential velocity `ω × r` when they separate, which your [physics_spec §8.2](docs/physics_spec.md) already specifies — that's the velocity effect, distinct from this pattern effect.)

## 2. Concave shapes

Two sub-questions hide here — *generating* them and how they *behave* — and the model treats them very differently from a traditional polygon engine.

### Generation: concavity is just "absent cells" — the same representation as damage

The prototype kept the outline convex only because clipping a Voronoi cell against a concave boundary is fiddly (Sutherland–Hodgman needs a convex clip window). But that's the wrong way to think about it in this model. **You don't clip cells to a concave outline — you define the shape *as* a set of cells.** Concavity emerges from which cells are present:

- Tessellate a region into convex Voronoi cells, then **remove** cells to carve a crater, a notch, a bay → the union is concave, and *every remaining cell is still a clean convex Voronoi cell*.
- For organic concave blobs, seed a non-convex region (a union of overlapping convex lobes, or a metaball field) and keep the cells whose seeds land inside.

The beautiful part: **a concave asteroid and a damaged asteroid are the same thing** — both are "a bond graph with some cells/bonds missing." An asteroid with a bite taken out of it is literally one that pre-fractured that region away. So concavity isn't a special geometry case; it's the base case. And because each cell stays convex, every geometry kernel you already have — half-plane clip, SAT, `ComputeInertia` — keeps operating on convex inputs only. That convex-cell / concave-union invariant is what makes the whole thing robust.

### Behaviour: concave features become *emergent* weak points

This is where the model genuinely outperforms the cut-based approach:

1. **Concave corners are stress concentrators — for free.** A cell sitting at the tip of a notch has fewer surrounding neighbours, so it has *fewer and weaker bonds*. Crack energy reaching it easily severs what little holds it. Real materials fail at notches (it's why you score glass before snapping); here it's automatic, because connectivity = bond count.

2. **Thin necks are topological weak points.** A peanut/dumbbell asteroid has a narrow ligament connecting two lobes. In graph terms that ligament's bonds are **bridges** (articulation points) — sever them and the connected-component step instantly hands you two separate bodies, for very little energy. "Shoot the neck and it splits in two" isn't scripted; it's just graph topology. You could even precompute articulation points to mark weak spots or drive enemy aim.

3. **Cracks route *around* holes.** Bonds only exist between physically adjacent cells, so a crack can't leap across an empty bay — it has to travel through connected material. Energy funnels into narrow ligaments and blows them, which is exactly right.

4. **Collision already works.** A concave asteroid is a `CompoundShape` of convex cells — your existing per-part SAT handles a bullet flying *into* a concave notch and striking an interior cell. No narrow-phase changes, which was the whole point of going compound.

5. **A fun side effect:** a concave body's centre of mass can sit in empty space (a crescent's CoM is out in its bay). The parallel-axis sum over cells handles this without caring, and it produces nice lopsided tumbling — and it means your rotation pre-stress field (above) is measured from a CoM that may not be inside the material, which is physically correct.

And to your opening intuition — this even scales past single asteroids: **a formation of asteroids "stuck together" is just one bond graph with weak inter-body bonds.** Same code shatters a cluster into its members and each member into cells. One representation, all the way up.

---

Both of these are things you'd want to *feel*, not just read. I can build a v2 of the prototype that adds:
- an **ω (spin) slider** + the centrifugal pre-stress field, so you can watch a spinning asteroid cleave into radial wedges and need less energy;
- a **directionality slider** so you can see the Hertzian forward-cone vs the isotropic splash;
- a **concave generator** (carve a crater / make a dumbbell) so you can shoot the neck and watch the connected-component split find it automatically.

Want me to extend the prototype with all three, or focus on the rotation pieces first so we don't muddy two variables at once?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:25 UTC

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:27 UTC

Clean. v2 is at [prototypes/fracture-cells-v2.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v2.html) (v1 untouched for comparison).

```bash
xdg-open /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/prototypes/fracture-cells-v2.html
```

## What's new

**Spin ω slider** — drives the centrifugal pre-stress field in `computePreStress()`. For every bond it computes the midpoint's radius from the centre of mass, its tangentiality (how circumferential it is), and weakens it by `Kspin · ω² · profile · tangentiality`. Crucially, **pre-stress is drawn on the bonds *before* you fire** — they shift green→red as you raise ω, so you can literally see which bonds are primed to fail. The auto-scaling ties the field to average bond strength, so it stays meaningful as you change toughness/grain.

**Directionality slider** — feeds the anisotropic delivery term in `propagate()`. Energy crossing a bond is scaled by `lerp(1, max(0,align)^1.6, directionality)`, where `align` is the dot product of the crack's local direction with the impact direction (inward, toward the CoM — drawn as a yellow arrow at the hit). At 0 it's the v1 isotropic splash; at 1 the damage punches a forward channel.

**Live re-firing** — dragging brittleness, toughness, spin, directionality, or energy re-runs the *same* hit (`refire()`), so the fracture pattern morphs under your hand. Option C's geometry re-split happens only on a fresh click, so sliders never runaway-subdivide. The stats box gained a **spin-primed** count (bonds pre-stressed past 50% of strength).

## The two experiments worth doing first

1. **Spin → wedges:** crank **Spin to 3.0**, watch the rim bonds glow red, then fire a *low-energy* shot. The body cleaves into radial pie-wedges that a static asteroid would have shrugged off — the grindstone-burst behaviour, emergent from pre-stress + connectivity, no special-case code.

2. **Directionality → channel:** set **Directionality 1.0**, **Spin 0**, and compare a dead-centre hit (punches a channel clear through) against a glancing edge hit (shears a sliver off the near side). Then drag the slider back to 0 and watch the same hit bloom into a round splash.

Combine them — spin up *and* high directionality — and you get a directional crack that gets deflected along the pre-stressed radial lines, which is a genuinely nice emergent interaction between the two axes.

One honest note carried over from v1: the impact direction here is the inward normal (toward CoM) because there's no real bullet trajectory in the harness — in the engine you'd use the actual bullet velocity, which lets glancing/oblique shots behave differently from head-on ones. Worth keeping in mind when you read the directionality behaviour.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:36 UTC

Clean. v3 is at [prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html) (v1 and v2 still intact).

```bash
xdg-open /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html
```

## What's new

**Shape dropdown** — Blob / Crater / Dumbbell / Crescent. The generation does the model-faithful thing in `makeAsteroid()`: it tessellates the *full* convex bounding region with Voronoi using **all** seeds, then **discards the cells whose seed fails `inShape()`**. That ordering matters — because each kept cell's bisectors with the now-removed neighbours still bound it, kept cells retain their true size and the removed region leaves clean gaps. Concavity is literally "absent cells," the same representation as fracture damage. No concave clipping anywhere; every cell stays convex.

**Impact angle slider** (−180°…180°) — `fractureFrom()` now takes the inward normal toward the centre of mass and rotates it by this offset. 0° = head-on, ±90° = grazing. It steers the directional forward cone, so it only bites when Directionality > 0. The yellow arrow at the impact shows the actual shot direction.

Both update live — the impact-angle slider re-fires the same hit, and the shape dropdown regenerates.

## The experiments that prove the concave claims

1. **Dumbbell → topological weak point:** pick **Dumbbell**, drop **energy to ~400**, and shoot the **neck**. The two lobes separate cleanly. Then shoot a lobe instead at the same energy — barely a chip. The neck is a *graph bridge*; the connected-component step finds it with no special-case code. Turn on **bonds** + **seeds** to see how thin the connecting bond column is.

2. **Crater → stress concentration:** pick **Crater**, hit right at the concave corner of the bay vs. out on the smooth convex back at the same energy. The concave corner fails earlier — cells there have fewer bonds (lower connectivity), so the crack severs what little holds them. Emergent notch-sensitivity.

3. **Crescent → CoM in empty space:** pick **Crescent**, add **Spin 2**, and watch the pre-stress field — the centre of mass sits in the bay (empty space), and the radial pre-stress and spin arc are drawn around that empty centre. The parallel-axis math doesn't care; it just produces interesting lopsided cleaving.

4. **Glancing shots:** any shape, **Directionality 1.0**, sweep **Impact angle** toward ±90°. A head-on shot punches inward; a grazing shot shears a sliver off the surface along the tangent — the difference between a direct hit and a ricochet-graze.

One honest caveat unchanged from before: boundary cells are clipped to the convex *bound*, not the ideal concave silhouette, so concave edges are slightly ragged at large grain — drop the grain for a cleaner outline. It doesn't affect the bond/connectivity behaviour, which is the point being demonstrated.

This is now a fairly complete design harness — it exercises every axis we've discussed (toughness, brittleness, grain, spin, directionality, impact angle, concavity, Option C). Reasonable next step would be to stop prototyping and capture all of this: I can write a **design-decision record** in `docs/` (the cell/bond model, where each parameter lives, why it beats the current `Split` approach, the open engineering tasks like concave-clip cleanliness and neck re-bonding), and/or sketch the **C# port** — `CellBody` component, bond graph, and a `FractureSystem` that replaces the current cut-based path. Want me to do the doc, the port sketch, or both?

## 🧑 User · 2026-05-31 13:50 UTC

I noticed that with more directionality the same impact loses more energy and ends up destroying less bonds. Does it make sense to propagate the same quantity of energy but bias it more toward the edges with similar direction to the impact trajectory?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:53 UTC

Sharp catch — and you've identified a genuine modeling flaw, not just a tuning quirk.

## Why it happens

Right now directionality is a pure **gain** on each bond:

```
deliver = e * lerp(1, max(0,align)^p, directionality)
```

That term is `≤ 1` for *every* bond except one pointing exactly along the impact. So as you raise directionality you're not redirecting energy — you're **throttling** it everywhere off-axis and giving nothing back. Directionality got conflated with attenuation. The only thing that should attenuate energy is `transmission` (brittleness) and the bond's own strength cost; directionality should change the *shape* of the stress field, not its total magnitude.

## Your instinct is exactly right

Conserve the drive, redistribute it toward aligned bonds. The fix is to **normalize the directional weights to a mean of 1** across each cell's intact outgoing bonds:

- At directionality 0 → all weights 1, normalization is a no-op → identical to before.
- At directionality 1 → forward bonds get *boosted above 1*, off-axis bonds starved toward 0, but the **sum is unchanged** — same total "drive" as an isotropic hit.

The nice consequence is that it's now physically right in *both* directions: a focused impact concentrates stress on-axis, so forward bonds should experience **more** stress than an isotropic hit of the same energy — they break that *deeper*, punching a channel, while the sides go quiet. That's the Hertzian behavior we were after, and it means raising directionality breaks *more* bonds along the axis instead of fewer everywhere.

Let me make the change in v3's `propagate()`:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 13:53 UTC

Done — reload [prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html). (v2 still has the old gain-only behavior if you want to A/B it.)

## What changed in `propagate()`

Instead of attenuating each bond independently, the cell now:
1. gathers its intact outgoing bonds and their alignment weights,
2. normalizes them to **mean 1** (`norm = bondCount / Σweights`), so the total delivered drive is independent of directionality,
3. delivers `e · min(w·norm, 3)` per bond — forward bonds boosted (capped at 3× to stay sane), off-axis starved.

Two guards keep it stable: the **3× cap** stops a lone forward bond from getting an absurd share, and the `tr ≤ e` clamp guarantees a child never ends up with more energy than its parent (no runaway amplification down a perfectly-focused channel). At directionality 0 the normalization is a no-op, so the isotropic case is byte-for-byte what it was.

## What you'll now see

Sweep directionality from 0 → 1 on a fixed hit and the **broken-bond count stays roughly flat** rather than collapsing — the breaks just *migrate* from a round splash into a forward channel. At high directionality the channel often drives *deeper* than the isotropic splash reached, because the concentrated drive pushes on-axis bonds well past threshold. That's the behavior you were expecting, and it's also more physically honest: focusing a fixed amount of energy concentrates stress, it doesn't dissipate it.

Worth noting one subtlety this exposes: brittleness (`transmission`) and directionality are now cleanly **orthogonal** — transmission controls how far the drive reaches per hop (decay), directionality controls *which* bonds it's spent on (shape). Before, they were tangled, because directionality was secretly also a decay. If you found brittleness behaved oddly at high directionality before, that's why; it should feel independent now.

This is a good correctness fix to fold into the eventual C# `FractureSystem` — the normalization is per-cell-visit and O(degree), so it's essentially free. Want me to capture the model (including this redistribution detail) in a design doc next, or keep poking at the prototype?

## 🧑 User · 2026-05-31 14:21 UTC

I noticed that the "broken-bond count" is actually now much higher when angularity is high

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 14:23 UTC

You're right, and I owe you a correction: my "stays flat" claim was wrong. Normalizing the *weights* doesn't conserve anything meaningful, because this flood model has no actual energy budget — each bond independently receives transmitted energy, so nothing gets "used up." Two things then inflate the count at high directionality:

1. **The boost** (up to 3×) pushes forward bonds that were marginal over their threshold → extra breaks.
2. **The `tr ≤ e` clamp** lets a boosted forward channel propagate at *constant* energy `e` without decaying, so the crack runs much deeper forward than the isotropic splash ever reached → a long chain of extra breaks.

So directionality is still secretly changing *how much* fractures, when it should only change *where*.

## The real fix: an energy budget

What you keep gesturing at — "the same quantity of energy" — is **conservation of fracture surface**. Breaking a bond costs energy (it creates new surface); a fixed impact energy should produce a fixed *total* amount of breakage, and directionality should only decide *which* bonds spend it. That's a budget, not a normalization.

So: give propagation a pool `budget = E`, and **subtract each broken bond's strength from it**; stop when it's empty. The flood still decides the *order* (highest-stress/most-aligned bonds break first), but the *total* is capped by the budget regardless of how it's steered. I also sort each cell's bonds by alignment so a limited budget is spent forward-first (clean channel) rather than on whatever iterated first.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 14:24 UTC

Reload [prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html).

## What you should see now

Sweep directionality 0 → 1 at a fixed energy and the **broken-bond count stays roughly constant** — the breaks just rearrange from a radial splash into a forward channel. At dir 0 the budget gets spent on the highest-stress bonds near the impact (radial); at dir 1 the per-cell sort + boost spend it forward-first, so the same total breakage drills a channel instead. Energy is now the only knob that sets *how much* breaks; directionality and impact angle set the *shape*.

## How the three knobs now decouple

- **Energy** → the budget → total fracture surface (how many bonds break).
- **Directionality / impact angle** → ordering → which bonds get the budget (channel vs splash).
- **Brittleness** → per-hop reach (how far the stress stays above threshold). At low brittleness a hit is *reach-limited* (ductile chip — stress dies before the budget is spent); at high brittleness it's *budget-limited* (the crack can reach far, so the budget is what stops it). That's the glass-vs-metal character, now cleanly separate from the other two.
- **Toughness / spin** → per-bond cost. Tougher bonds cost more budget so fewer break; spin pre-stress lowers cost so a spinning body breaks more for the same energy — both consistent.

One honest note: `budget = E` with cost = bond strength is an arbitrary unit choice, so the absolute count is just "energy ÷ average bond strength." That's fine for the prototype — drag the energy slider to taste — but in the C# port you'd calibrate the budget against real impact energy (and ideally subtract the kinetic energy that fragments fly away with, so surface + kinetic ≈ input — true conservation). Worth flagging because that kinetic split is the one piece of real energy accounting the prototype still fakes (separation velocities are cosmetic).

This budget model is, I think, the keystone the whole design was missing — it's what makes "place and force determine the break" literally true: force sets the budget, place-and-direction set where it's spent. Good instinct pushing on it. Want me to capture the model now (design doc with the budget/propagation/spin/directionality rules and the open calibration questions), or keep refining?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 14:59 UTC

Good — let me lay out the organizing principle first, because it answers most of your "engine vs game" question, then give the checklist.

## The boundary principle

Two lines cleanly separate what belongs where:

> **The engine owns the *physics* of destruction. The game owns the *meaning* of what breaks.**

The engine knows about cells, bonds, energy, stress propagation, fragments, mass, inertia, collision. It does **not** know what an "asteroid" is, what "rock" vs "ice" means, what a score is, or that small fragments are worth points. Those are game concepts.

> **The engine never references a UI toolkit.** It draws through an abstraction; SDL+Skia, WinForms+GDI+, and a web canvas are interchangeable adapters behind it.

Everything below is just the disciplined application of those two lines.

## Responsibility map

| Concern | Engine | Game |
|---|---|---|
| ECS, world, game loop, **fixed timestep** | ✅ | |
| Rigid-body dynamics, collision, contacts, **raycast/CCD** | ✅ | |
| **Destruction module** (the engine's signature) | ✅ | |
| — cell tessellation (Voronoi via half-plane clip) | ✅ | seeds, grain, shape membership = game/content |
| — bond graph structure + `FracturableBody` component | ✅ | |
| — fracture simulation (budget, brittleness, directionality, spin pre-stress, connected components) | ✅ | |
| — energy accounting incl. **kinetic/surface split** | ✅ | material constants = game |
| — emits a `FractureResult` (broken bonds, separated components, fragment geometry+mass+inertia+velocity) | ✅ | |
| Turning fragments into *entities* (assign gameplay components, score, tags) | | ✅ |
| Material catalog (rock/ice/metal presets), wave→material map | | ✅ |
| Asteroid shapes/grain authoring, concave carving choices | | ✅ |
| Bullets as a weapon, enemy AI, waves, HUD, leaderboard | | ✅ |
| `IRenderer` abstraction + Camera + render systems | ✅ | what to draw / colors |
| Platform adapters (SDL/Skia, WinForms/GDI+) | separate assemblies | |

The key reused pattern (already in your [physics_spec §10.1](docs/physics_spec.md)): **the fracture system computes everything and emits an event; the game wires entities.** That stays — it's exactly why the engine can be destruction-specialized without knowing about asteroids.

## On the kinetic-energy split (your flag)

This lives in the engine's fracture sim, and it's what closes the conservation loop:

```
E_effective  =  E_surface  +  E_kinetic  +  E_loss
```

- `E_surface` = the **budget** that breaks bonds (Σ broken bond strengths) — what we just built.
- `E_kinetic` = the KE the fragments carry off as they separate (Σ ½·m·v_spread²).
- `E_loss` = heat/sound (a dissipation coefficient).

The prototype fakes `E_kinetic` (cosmetic spread speeds). The honest version makes the two *share one pool*: reserve a fraction of `E_effective` for kinetic, derive fragment spread speeds from that share and their masses (lighter → faster, automatically), and the **remainder is the fracture budget**. Brittle materials put more into surface (more cracks, less fling); ductile put more into kinetic (fewer cracks, bigger shove). That coupling is itself a material-character knob. I'd spec the coefficient form first, with the closed-loop ("measure actual fragment KE, subtract from budget") as a refinement.

## Missing engine features — checklist to confirm

**Loop & time**
- [ ] Fixed timestep with accumulator + render interpolation (currently variable, [GameLoop.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Core/GameLoop.cs)). Needed once fragments stack and contacts resolve.
- [ ] Deterministic seeded RNG threaded through fracture (for replays/leaderboard integrity).

**Collision & contacts**
- [ ] **Raycast / shape-cast query** against the spatial index + shapes (anti-tunnelling; enables raycast bullets).
- [ ] Contact solver with a few iterations (stacking fragments don't jitter/explode).
- [ ] Body **sleeping/deactivation** (perf — a field full of settled fragments).
- [ ] Friction (currently only restitution on `RigidBody`).
- [ ] **Internal broad-phase inside `CompoundShape`** ([CompoundShape.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs) is O(parts) per test) — the real perf cliff as cells multiply.

**Destruction module (new — the bulk of the spec)**
- [ ] Cell tessellation utility (Voronoi by half-plane clipping; concave = cell membership).
- [ ] Bond graph + `FracturableBody` component (cells, adjacency, strengths, pre-stress).
- [ ] `FractureSimulator`: energy model, budget, propagation (brittleness transmission, directionality redistribution, spin pre-stress), connected components, fragment geometry/mass/inertia, kinetic split.
- [ ] **Compound re-fracture path** (today `FragmentAsteroid` returns early on a `CompoundShape` — [Program.cs:179](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs#L179); the model makes this the normal case).
- [ ] Optional Option-C cell re-split.
- [ ] `FractureResult`/event contract.

**Rendering & UI abstraction (the WinForms requirement)**
- [ ] `IRenderer` immediate-mode primitive interface (DrawPolygon/Circle/Line/Fill, push/pop transform) in the engine; [RenderSystem.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/RenderSystem.cs) and a polygon/compound render system target it.
- [ ] Decouple [Camera.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Rendering/Camera.cs) and render systems from Skia/SDL (the asteroid demo currently draws Skia directly).
- [ ] Backend adapters as separate assemblies: SDL+Skia (now), **WinForms+GDI+** (future), both behind `IRenderer` + the existing [IGameWindow.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Rendering/IGameWindow.cs).

**Hygiene**
- [ ] Object pooling for fragments/debris (GC pressure under heavy destruction).
- [ ] Geometry invariant tests (area/centroid/convexity/connectivity).

**Explicitly cut/deferred:** the §5 parallelism taxonomy (`ISystemMetadata`, wave scheduler, double-buffered stores) — premature at your entity counts; it's documentation debt competing for attention with the destruction work that's actually your differentiator.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 15:17 UTC

Three good challenges. Let me answer them honestly — the third and first actually reshape the skeleton — then write the spec.

## 1. Parallelism — you're right that the picture changed, and I'll revise, but the *target* moves

Here's the key thing I should have separated earlier: **cells are not entities.** A 100-cell asteroid is **one** entity with a 100-part compound collider. So "dozens of asteroids × 100 cells" is still only *dozens of rigid bodies*. Cells don't integrate, don't move independently, don't iterate in the ECS. That means the systems §5 obsesses over — `PhysicsSystem`, `MovementSystem` iterating entities with `ForEachParallel` — operate on dozens of bodies. Threading those is still pointless.

So where does the cost actually go? Recomputed for your scale:

| Cost | Driver | First fix |
|---|---|---|
| **Collision narrow phase** | compound-vs-compound is O(partsA × partsB) — two 100-cell asteroids = 10,000 SAT tests | **algorithmic** (internal broad-phase per compound) → ~O(parts) |
| Fracture sim | only on impact, bounded by the energy budget | amortize / off-thread the spike |
| Rendering | thousands of convex polys/frame | cull + batch (backend concern) |
| Body integration | dozens–hundreds of bodies | trivial, single-thread |

So my revised position: **the speculative system-scheduler taxonomy (the 7 models, `ISystemMetadata`, wave scheduler, double-buffered stores) stays cut** — it targets entity-iteration that doesn't exist at scale here. But **parallelism is worth it in exactly one place: the collision narrow phase** (a `Parallel.For` over candidate pairs / over cells within a compound). That's the genuinely defensible part of the old §5.9.

Crucially, the **order matters**: the internal-broad-phase algorithmic fix gives a bigger win than threads *and is mandatory anyway*, so it comes first; parallelizing the narrow phase is a profiling-gated optimization layered on top. And there's a real tension to spec: **parallelism vs determinism** — you want fixed-step + seeded RNG for leaderboard integrity, so any parallel narrow phase must write into deterministic per-pair slots, not order-dependent accumulators. I'll add a "Concurrency & determinism" section that states this scoped position rather than the blanket "cut it."

## 2. Input — yes, it enters through the UI, but `InputSystem` stays platform-free

Correct. The OS event pump lives in the platform/UI layer (SDL poll, WinForms `KeyDown` on the UI thread). The clean layering:

```
Platform adapter (SDL window / WinForms Form)
   → pushes OS events, mapped to engine KeyCode, into a thread-safe input snapshot
Engine InputSystem
   → reads the abstracted snapshot, maintains Held / PressedThisFrame / ReleasedThisFrame
```

`InputSystem` never sees an SDL or WinForms type — it consumes the abstracted feed (your `KeyCode` enum already does this). The coupling is *only* at the adapter boundary. The one nuance to spec: the adapter must marshal input into a snapshot the engine samples once per fixed step (WinForms fires on a different thread than the loop), so the handoff is thread-safe.

## 3. Resource manager + audio — yes, missing, and they belong to the same boundary

Good catch. And it reframes §3 cleanly: rendering, input, **assets, and audio** are all the *same kind of thing* — platform-specific capabilities the engine uses through an interface. So I'll introduce a **Platform Abstraction Layer (PAL)** as the umbrella:

| PAL interface | Engine side | SDL backend | WinForms backend |
|---|---|---|---|
| `IRenderer` | draw primitives, transforms | Skia | GDI+ |
| input feed | `KeyCode`, snapshot | SDL poll | Form events |
| `IResourceManager` | id→handle cache, ref-count | SKImage/decode | `Bitmap`/`Font` |
| `IAudioBackend` | `Play(id,vol,pan)`, music | OpenAL/SDL_mixer | NAudio |
| window/timing | fixed-step loop host | SDL window | Form + loop |

The engine core + destruction module depend only on PAL **interfaces**; each backend is a separate assembly. WinForms becomes "just another PAL implementation," which is exactly the guarantee you wanted. The resource manager keeps the caching/ref-counting logic engine-side ([ResourceManager.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Resources/ResourceManager.cs) exists but is GDI+-coupled — it gets split into engine-side cache + backend-side loader).

## Updated skeleton (deltas only)

- §3 becomes **Platform Abstraction Layer** — 3.2 `IRenderer`, 3.3 input feed, 3.4 `IResourceManager`, 3.5 `IAudioBackend`, 3.6 window/timing.
- New **§3.7 Concurrency & determinism** — scoped parallel narrow phase, gated by profiling, deterministic; explicitly *not* the system-scheduler taxonomy.
- §3.x keeps fixed timestep, raycast, contact solver, compound internal broad-phase.

Everything else stands. Writing it now.

## What's in it

- **§2 Boundary principles** — the two rules (engine = physics of destruction; engine never touches a UI toolkit) and the full responsibility map.

- **§10 Roadmap** — Phase A (PAL) → B (loop/physics) → C (destruction) → D (game integration) → E (game) → F (optimisation), with the WinForms note that it's just a backend.

## Two things worth your eye

- **§4.5 (kinetic/surface split)** is the one part that's newly formalised beyond the prototype — the prototype faked fragment KE, and this makes spread speeds *derive* from the conserved kinetic share. The `E_impact + E_spin ≈ E_surface + E_kinetic + E_loss` identity is the spec's backbone; if that partition feels off, it's the highest-leverage thing to adjust.

## 🧑 User · 2026-05-31 18:50 UTC

What do they do and why are they needed

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 18:52 UTC

Good questions — these two items look like generic physics-engine boilerplate, but they're each here for a specific reason tied to *your* destruction model. Let me frame the distinction first, because it explains why both exist:

> Collision has two regimes. **Impacts** — two things hit and bounce apart — which your engine already handles. And **resting contacts** — things that come to lie against each other and stay there. Your current code only does impacts well. The destruction model produces *lots* of resting contacts (a shattered asteroid is a heap of fragments touching each other), so Phase B fills that gap before Phase C creates the heaps.

---

## Phase B: raycast / shape-cast query

### What it is
A query against the physics world: *"cast a ray (or sweep a shape) from point A to point B — what's the first thing it hits, exactly where, and what's the surface normal?"* It returns `{ entity, hitPoint, normal, t, partIndex }`. A **ray** is infinitely thin; a **shape-cast** sweeps a circle/polygon along the path (for projectiles with thickness).

### Why it's needed — the tunnelling problem
Right now bullets are physics bodies — little circles that move by `position += velocity·dt` each step and get caught by the discrete overlap check in [CollisionSystem.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs). At a fixed 1/120 s step, a fast bullet jumps a chunk of space per step. If that jump is bigger than the target is thick, the bullet is *behind* the target on one step and *past* it on the next — it never overlaps, so the collision is never detected. The bullet flies straight through. That's **tunnelling**.

Your code already half-fights this: [`FragmentAsteroid`](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs) calls `PolygonUtils.NearestPointOnBoundary` to drag the contact point back onto the surface *after* a fuzzy hit. That's a patch on a symptom.

A raycast fixes the cause. Each frame, treat the bullet's travel as a **segment** and ask the world what it crosses first — analytically, independent of step size. Three payoffs:

1. **No tunnelling, ever.** The segment can't skip over a target; intersection is exact math.
2. **Exact impact data for free.** The fracture model needs a precise surface point, the surface normal, and *which cell* was struck (`partIndex`). A raycast hands you all three exactly. The body-collision path gives a fuzzy point that may even be *inside* the polygon after tunnelling.
3. **Cheaper.** A ray bullet isn't a rigid body — no integration, no collision-pair generation, no impulse. For a bullet-heavy game that's a real saving.

### How it plugs in
Reuse the existing broad phase: the `SpatialGrid` finds which cells' AABBs the segment crosses; then a new `RayIntersect` on `PolygonShape`/`CircleShape` does the closed-form ray-vs-convex test; keep the nearest `t`. The **shape-cast** variant (for a grenade or thick slug) sweeps a circle by testing the ray against the target inflated by the projectile radius. You confirmed raycast bullets earlier — guns become rays, while physical things like grenades can stay bodies and use shape-cast.

---

## Phase B: contact solver iterations, friction, sleeping

These three are the **resting-contact trilogy**. Today, per collision, [CollisionSystem.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs) does one positional push (`TrySeparate`) + one velocity impulse (`TryApplyImpulse`) — a single pass. That's fine for sparse bouncing. It falls apart the moment many bodies touch at once — which is exactly what a shatter creates.

### (a) Solver iterations
**What:** resolve *all* contacts together, repeating for several passes (4–8) per step, instead of each contact once.

**Why:** contacts are **coupled**. Picture a fresh shatter — a dozen fragments momentarily overlapping and pushing apart. Fixing fragment A↔B shoves B into C, which shoves C into D… A single pass can't find a state where *all* the overlaps are resolved at once. The result is the classic artifact: fragments interpenetrate, jitter, or **pop apart explosively**. Iterating (sequential impulses, the Box2D approach) converges toward a consistent solution — each pass shrinks the leftover error, and after a handful the cluster resolves cleanly. It's what makes a shatter look like rock breaking instead of popcorn exploding.

### (b) Friction
**What:** a *tangential* impulse opposing sliding at a contact, capped by the Coulomb limit (≤ μ × normal impulse). Your `RigidBody` currently has only `Restitution` (bounciness — the normal direction). Friction is the missing sideways half.

**Why:** without it, every surface is frictionless ice. Fragments slide and spin forever and can't grind to a halt; a pile of debris slumps and slides apart instead of holding. Friction is also a **material knob** that ties into the destruction model — rock fragments should grind and stop, ice should slither. It's what makes "rough rock" feel different from "glass."

**Honest caveat for your game:** in a zero-gravity toroidal field there's no ground and no gravity pulling piles together, so friction matters *less* here than in a platformer. Its main value is fragment-on-fragment grinding and material feel. It's cheap to add, so worth having, but it's the least critical of the three for a space game.

### (c) Sleeping (deactivation)
**What:** a body whose linear+angular speed stays below a threshold for a moment is marked **asleep** and skipped by integration and the solver until something touches it or pushes it.

**Why — performance.** A destruction-heavy scene can leave hundreds of fragments lying around. Simulating every one each step (integrate, generate pairs, run N solver iterations) is wasted work when they're barely moving. Sleeping makes cost scale with what's *actually active*, not total entity count. At your scale this is a bigger perf lever than parallelism. It also kills the faint residual shimmer that iterative solvers leave on resting bodies — asleep means perfectly still.

**Honest caveat for your game:** sleeping triggers on near-zero velocity. In space, a fragment can drift at constant velocity *forever* and never slow down — so it'd never sleep. It only works because your bodies have `LinearDrag`/`AngularDrag`, which eventually brings them near rest. So sleeping helps the *settling* debris, but in a near-frictionless space sim you also lean on what you already do — TTL/culling of debris and the compound broad-phase — for the drifting-but-not-resting case.

---

## Why this is Phase B (before the destruction in Phase C)

The destruction model is a *producer* of exactly the hard cases these features handle: a single shot turns one body into many simultaneously-contacting fragments that then settle. If you build Phase C first, the very first shatter would look like jittering popcorn on ice — interpenetrating, popping, sliding forever. Phase B lays the floor so that when fragments start flying in Phase C, they collide, settle, and rest believably, and a field full of them stays cheap.

Ranked by impact for *your* zero-g destruction game: **raycast bullets** (correctness — no more tunnelling, exact fracture impact) and **solver iterations** (clean shatters, no popping) are the big wins; **sleeping** is the main perf lever for debris; **friction** is the nice-to-have that adds material feel. That's also a sensible order to build them in — want me to start with raycast next?

## What was added

**Engine (the query):**
- [RayCastResult.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Collision/RayCastResult.cs) — shape-level hit (distance, point, normal, partIndex).
- `Raycast` on each shape: **circle** (ray-sphere quadratic), **polygon** (Cyrus-Beck clip of the ray against the convex half-planes, returns the pierced face's outward normal), **compound** (nearest part, records `PartIndex`). Default `false` on the base so `AABBShape` needs no change.
- [PhysicsQueries.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/PhysicsQueries.cs) — `Raycast(world, from, to, layerMask, out RayHit)`: nearest collider hit on a matching layer, tightening the max distance as it scans.

**Demo (the payoff — bullets are now rays):**
- Bullets lost their `Collider`/`RigidBody`; they're just a moving visual now.
- `RaycastBulletSystem` sweeps each bullet's **per-step travel segment** (`PreviousPosition → Position`) against asteroids and publishes a `BulletHitEvent`; `DemoSession` routes that to `FragmentAsteroid` with the **exact** hit point.
- Placed **after MovementSystem, before WrapSystem**, so the swept segment is one continuous step — never a wrap-teleport across the screen. Fresh bullets spawn with `PreviousPosition = spawnAt` (zero first segment, no false hit).
- `FragmentAsteroid` now trusts the raycast point instead of snapping from the bullet body's possibly-tunneled position.

## Why this matters

The tunnelling bug is **structurally gone** — a bullet can't skip past an asteroid between steps because the ray covers the whole gap. And the fracture system now gets a precise surface point + normal + struck cell index, which is exactly what the Phase C destruction model consumes.

## What changed

**`RigidBody`** gained `Friction`, `SleepTimer`, `Asleep`.

**`PhysicsSystem`** skips asleep bodies during integration, and `ApplyForce`/`ApplyForceAtPoint` now **wake** the body (so the ship can park, sleep, and re-thrust without getting stuck).

**`CollisionSystem`** was restructured from one-shot resolution into the standard pipeline:
1. **Gather** a `Contact` per dynamic pair (precomputing effective normal/tangent masses and the restitution bias), keeping the existing positional `TrySeparate`.
2. **Iterative solve** (`Iterations = 6`): sequential impulses — **normal** impulse (accumulated, clamped ≥ 0) then **Coulomb friction** (clamped to ±μ·normalImpulse). Iterating is what lets coupled/stacked contacts converge instead of jittering or popping.
3. **Sleeping**: bodies below the velocity thresholds for 0.5 s deactivate (velocity zeroed); contacts and forces wake them.

The math is identical to the old single impulse on the first iteration (I verified the `-(1+e)·vn₀` formula falls out), so isolated bounces feel the same — the iterations only add stability for the *many-simultaneous-contact* case that Phase C's shatter debris will create.

## Backward-compatible by default

- **Friction defaults to 0**, combined as `√(fA·fB)` — so asteroids (which don't set it) behave exactly as before. Set `RigidBody.Friction` to opt in (rock = high, ice = low, tying into the material model later).
- **Fast asteroids never sleep** (they stay above 6 px/s), so the demo looks unchanged; only the idle ship will sleep, and it wakes on thrust.

## To verify visually

```bash
cd GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo && dotnet run
```
Bounces should feel the same or slightly steadier; nothing should jitter or explode. Park the ship a moment then thrust — it should respond instantly (sleep/wake working).

- **Single-point contacts:** the narrow phase returns one contact point per pair. That's fine for zero-g scatter/bounce, but rock-solid *flat resting stacks* would want 2-point contact **manifolds** — a deferred solver-quality refinement, not needed for a gravity-free space game.
- **TwoPlayerGame** shares `CollisionSystem`, so its balls now use the iterative solver + sleeping too. If a slowly-drifting ball ever freezes oddly, that demo can set `EnableSleeping = false` on its collision system. (Didn't touch it; flagging in case.)

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 22:09 UTC

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-05-31 22:09 UTC

Compound broad-phase done — and with it, **Phase B is complete**. Both projects build 0/0.

## What changed in `CompoundShape`

- **Precomputed per-cell local AABBs** (once, in the constructor) plus their union.
- Every collision fan-out (`Intersects`, `IntersectsCircle/Polygon/AABB`) now transforms the *other* shape's world AABB into the compound's local frame and **skips cells that can't overlap** before the expensive SAT. A 100-cell asteroid is now a linear scan of cheap AABB tests + a few SAT tests, not 100 SAT tests — and compound-vs-compound drops from O(partsA × partsB) toward O(k_A × k_B) since both sides cull.
- **`GetAABB` is now O(1)** — it transforms the four corners of the precomputed local-bounds box instead of re-transforming every vertex of every cell each frame (which was O(parts × verts)).

The culling is **conservative** (the local query box is a superset of the true footprint), so it never wrongly skips an overlapping cell — results are identical to before, just computed far faster. `LastHitPartIndex` still reports the deepest-contact cell correctly. This is a pure perf change with no behavioral difference, so nothing visual should change in the demo.

## Calibration — read this first

This is the spec §9 open question made tangible. With the **defaults, every hit full-shatters** — at Energy ×1 the impact energy (~80k) dwarfs the asteroid's total bond strength (~20k). To get *chunks* instead of dust, the first thing to do is:

- drop **Energy ×** toward ~0.2–0.4, **or** raise **Toughness** (then press **R**), and
- play **Brittleness** (live): high → shatters wide; low → local chips.
- **Directionality** to 1.0 + a glancing shot → watch the forward channel; **spin** (Asteroid spin `[R]`) high → radial cleaving.

Once you find values that *feel* right, those are the numbers we bake into the material presets (and I can flip the energy/budget into properly calibrated absolute units so Energy × can go back to 1.0).

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-01 08:18 UTC

The collisions are not working properly, fragments "absorb" each other and spin uncontrollably

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:21 UTC

Good catch — that's a real bug, and I know the cause. Every asteroid is now a multi-cell `CompoundShape`, but the collision pipeline returns only **one** contact point per body pair (the single deepest cell). Two overlapping asteroids get one contact at a rim vertex, so: (a) the single push can't resolve the multi-cell overlap → they "absorb," and (b) that rim contact point's large lever arm + the contact jumping between cells each frame injects torque → uncontrollable spin. This is the contact-manifold gap I flagged as deferred — it's now biting because *everything* is a compound.

The fix: generate a **manifold** (one contact per overlapping cell pair) and use gentle positional correction instead of a full-depth teleport. Let me add manifold collection to `CompoundShape`:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:22 UTC

Now wire the manifold into `CollisionSystem` and soften the positional correction (no more full-depth teleport):

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:22 UTC

Now add the consts, the pair-collection helper, and soften `TrySeparate`:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:23 UTC

Both build clean. Here's what I changed to fix it:

## Root cause
Every asteroid is now a multi-cell `CompoundShape`, but collision resolution used a **single** contact point per pair. Two overlapping asteroids → one contact at a rim vertex →
- one push can't clear a multi-cell overlap → they **interpenetrate ("absorb")**, and
- a rim contact has a long lever arm, and the "deepest cell" jumps frame-to-frame → **torque injected → runaway spin**.

## The fix (engine-side, benefits everything)
1. **Contact manifolds** — `CompoundShape.CollectContacts` now returns one contact *per overlapping cell pair*, and `CollisionSystem` feeds *all* of them to the iterative solver. Multiple balanced contact points resolve the overlap and cancel the spurious torque (and friction now acts at several points, damping spin).
2. **Gentle positional correction** — `TrySeparate` no longer teleports bodies apart by the full penetration depth (which flings/spins them). It now fixes ~40% of the penetration beyond a 0.5px slop per frame (standard Baumgarte), so deep overlaps resolve smoothly over a few frames.

This is the contact-manifold work I'd flagged as deferred — it became necessary the moment every body became a compound.

```bash
cd GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo && dotnet run
```

Please give it another drive. Asteroids should now **bounce off each other cleanly** and fragments should separate without the wild spin or merging. 

If there's *still* more spin than feels right, the next levers are: better contact points (clip-based per-cell manifold instead of the deepest-vertex approximation), or a small angular-damping term — but the manifold + soft correction is the standard fix and should resolve what you saw. Tell me how it behaves.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-01 08:27 UTC

It didn't solve the problem: the fragments (and also the whole asteroids) trap each other and start spinning while inside each other. Something's wrong with the collision system

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:30 UTC

It didn't work because the manifold wasn't the real bug — and now the symptom ("pushing *into* each other") points right at it: **the contact normal is flipped** for polygon-polygon (and therefore compound) collisions.

Tracing the SAT path: `cA.Intersects(cB)` returns a normal pointing **A→B** (toward the other body), but `TrySeparate` and the impulse solver expect **B→A** (the direction A must move to *separate*). So separation pushes them *together*, and the impulse solver sees approaching bodies as "already separating" and applies nothing → they sink in and grind/spin. The circle paths handle the dispatch sign correctly, which is why the player (circle) seemed fine but asteroid-vs-asteroid (polygon/compound) is broken. With single PolygonShape asteroids before, this was rarely exercised; now every body is a compound of polygons, so it's everywhere.

The robust fix: orient every contact normal by the bodies' relative position so it always points B→A, regardless of the shape code's internal sign:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 08:31 UTC

Builds clean. This was the actual bug — a latent flipped-normal in the polygon SAT path that the all-compound design exposed everywhere.

```bash
cd GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo && dotnet run
```

With the normal now correctly pointing B→A:
- **`TrySeparate` pushes bodies apart** instead of together, so they stop trapping/sinking.
- **The impulse solver now sees the approach correctly** (it was misreading approaching bodies as separating and doing nothing), so they actually bounce.
- The runaway spin should largely go away too, because it was being fed by the persistent overlap — once bodies separate cleanly each frame, there's no overlap to inject energy.

Please give it another run — asteroids and fragments should now collide and push apart properly rather than merging and spinning.

Two honest follow-ups depending on what you see:
- If there's still *some* excess spin on glancing hits, the next refinement is better **contact points** (the SAT contact point is a single approximate vertex; a clip-based per-cell contact would place it correctly) and/or a touch of angular damping. But that's polish on top of correct behavior.
- The deeper cause was that `PolygonShape`'s SAT returns the normal in the opposite sign to the engine's documented "B→A" convention. I fixed it robustly at the `CollisionSystem` level (orient by relative position, which also self-corrects any shape that's already correct). If you'd prefer, I can instead fix it at the source in `SatPolygonPolygon` so the shape contract itself is correct — cleaner, but a slightly higher-blast-radius change. Let me know which you prefer once you've confirmed the behavior is right.

```
DEFAULT     E= 80,999   threshold= 28,649   sumBonds= 20,475   => 22 frags (full shatter)
MIN-BUL     E=      10   threshold= 28,649                     => 0 frags (no fracture)
MAXtuf      E=      10   threshold=1,278,963                   => 0 frags
MAX-BUL     E=1,195,842,688                                    => full shatter
```

Two problems jump out:
1. **The energy magnitude is ~4× too high at default**: `E=81,000` vs `sumBonds=20,475`. Once impact energy exceeds the *total* bond strength, every bond can break → full shatter. So the default bullet is already way past full-shatter.
2. **`E ∝ v²` gives an absurd dynamic range** — 10 to 1.2 *billion* across the speed slider. The useful window (partial fracture) is a razor-thin sliver, so you're almost always either below threshold (nothing) or far above (full shatter).

Also — and this explains your "max toughness still shatters": **Toughness is an `[R]` param**, baked at spawn. If you didn't press R, the on-screen asteroids still have the old toughness, so the slider did nothing live.
