## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 09:05 UTC

Now — every parameter explained. First the mental model, because the parameters only make sense against it:

## How a hit decides what breaks

1. A bullet deposits **energy** `E ≈ ½ · mass · speed²` into the struck cell.
2. If `E` is below a **threshold** (≈ toughness × the struck cell's mass), nothing breaks — it just chips and *accumulates* (repeated taps eventually add up).
3. Above threshold, a **budget** `= Fracture-eff × (E − threshold)` is spent **breaking bonds**, starting at the struck cell and spreading outward.
4. **Brittleness** sets how far the crack reaches, **directionality** steers which way, **spin** pre-weakens bonds. Whatever cells end up disconnected fly off as **fragments**; the rest stays.

So: **energy** decides *how much* breaks, **toughness/Fracture-eff** decide *how hard it is*, and **brittleness/directionality/spin** decide *the pattern*.

## The parameters

### Impact energy — "how much breaks"
| Param | Effect |
|---|---|
| **Bullet speed** | Energy ∝ speed² — the dominant, *quadratic* energy control. Small changes swing breakage a lot. |
| **Bullet mass** | Energy ∝ mass (linear) + bigger momentum shove. |
| **Energy ×** | A plain multiplier on bullet energy — quick "more/less power" master. |
| **Fracture eff.** ⭐(new) | Fraction of available energy that actually creates cracks (rest dissipates as heat/sound). **This is your master "shatter amount" dial.** Low → chips; high → full shatter. This is what was missing — raw energy was 100% efficient, so everything maxed out. |
| **Fire cooldown** | Just rate of fire — not destruction. |

### Pattern — "which way it breaks"
| Param | Effect |
|---|---|
| **Directionality** | 0 = isotropic round splash; 1 = forward cone/channel along the shot. Steers the crack, doesn't change how much. |
| **Spin energy frac** | How much of a spinning asteroid's rotational energy counts as fracture pre-stress. Higher → fast-spinning rocks shatter easier and **cleave into radial wedges**. |
| **Momentum xfer** | How hard the fragments get knocked in the bullet's travel direction (the "shove"). |

### Material — live, applies to all asteroids
| Param | Effect |
|---|---|
| **Brittleness** | 0 = ductile (crack stays *local* → chip near impact); 1 = glass (crack travels *far* → wide shatter). Controls crack **reach** and biases energy toward more cracks. |
| **Kinetic frac** | Split between *flinging* fragments vs *cracking*. High → fragments fly fast but fewer pieces; low → more pieces, gentler spread. |
| **Min frag area** | Pieces smaller than this become **debris particles** (no collision) instead of real chunks. Higher → more dust. |
| **Toughness** ⭐(now live) | Bond strength + fracture threshold = material **hardness**. Higher → harder to break, fewer pieces. *(Was `[R]`/baked — that's why maxing it did nothing live before; now it rescales every bond each frame.)* |

### Body physics — live, all bodies
| Param | Effect |
|---|---|
| **Restitution** | Bounciness, 0 (dead) → 1 (super-bouncy). |
| **Friction** | Contact grip — grinds down sliding/spin on contact; 0 = ice. |
| **Linear drag / Angular drag** | How fast bodies slow / stop spinning (space "thickness"). 0 = drift forever. |
| **Thrust** | Player ship acceleration. |

### Spawn-time `[R]` — press R to apply
| Param | Effect |
|---|---|
| **Asteroids / Radius / Speed / Spin** | How many, how big, initial drift speed, initial spin. |
| **Grain** | Target **cell size**. Smaller grain → more, finer cells → finer possible shatter (and more collision cost). Larger → chunkier. |
| **Density** | Mass per area → heavier (more inertia, harder to push, raises the fracture threshold). |

## Two fixes I made
- **`Fracture eff.` coefficient** (default 0.12): brings the fracture budget into the bond-strength range, so a default hit gives a few chunks instead of instant full shatter — and gives you a smooth dial from chip → shatter.
- **Toughness is now live**: it rescales every bond from its stored edge length each frame, so the slider responds immediately (no more pressing R and seeing no effect).

## Suggested starting point to feel it
Leave defaults, then sweep **just `Fracture eff.`** from 0.02 → 0.4: you'll watch the same shot go from a single chip to a full shatter. Then **Brittleness** 0 vs 1 to feel local-chip vs wide-shatter, and **Directionality** to 1.0 with a glancing shot for the forward channel. **Toughness** is your other big dial now that it's live.

Rebuild and give it a drive — it should finally be *controllable* across the whole range.

Some things I noticed:

2. Angularity feels wrong: I get the expected behaviour (energy propagating more in the direction of the hit) only by maximizing angularity. 

The main one is spin energy frac, if I make it = 0, practically no hit has any force and gets completely absorbed. I think this problem dwarfes the others and may be the root cause for why it feels so wrong.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 10:04 UTC

Brilliant diagnosis — you've found the real root cause, and it explains all three symptoms. Let me show you the numbers:

**`E_spin = SpinEnergyFraction × ½ · I · ω²`**. For a default asteroid: inertia `I ≈ ½·mass·r² ≈ ½·43000·110² ≈ 2.6×10⁸`. At a gentle spin of ω=0.6: `½Iω² ≈ 47,000,000`. Times 0.35 = **~16 million** units of "fracture energy" — versus a default bullet's **~81,000**. So the spin term is **~200× the bullet's entire energy**, sitting on every spinning asteroid. That's why:

1. **Everything full-shatters** — the budget is unlimited (16M ≫ total bond strength ~20k), so *every* bond breaks regardless of your bullet/toughness settings. Raising density makes it *worse* (heavier → bigger inertia → bigger E_spin).
2. **Directionality only works at max** — with an unlimited budget, every bond breaks anyway, so steering is invisible. It only appeared when you starved the energy enough (your workaround) to make the budget actually *limited*.
3. **Spin-frac = 0 → everything absorbed** — removing the 16M leaves only the (tiny, at your low settings) bullet energy, which is below threshold.

The bug: spin's rotational KE is genuinely *enormous* relative to a bullet, so adding it to the impact energy is catastrophically mis-scaled. **Spin should *pre-stress* (weaken) the bonds — making a spinning rock easier to shatter and cleave radially — not dump millions of energy into the budget.** That pre-stress mechanism already exists in the propagation and is properly bounded; the additive energy term is the mistake.

The fix: **remove the additive `E_spin`** entirely; the impact energy is just the bullet's. Repurpose the slider to control the (bounded) spin *pre-stress* instead.

Confirmed fixed. The numbers now behave exactly as they should:

```
speed 900, ω=0.0  → 5 frags    (default partial break)
speed 900, ω=0.6  → 5 frags    (gentle spin barely matters — no more domination)
speed 900, ω=3.0  → 10 frags   (fast spin shatters more, but bounded)
speed 400, ω=0.6  → none       (controllable low end)
speed 600, ω=0.6  → 1 chip
speed 600, ω=3.0, pre-stress 2.0 → 14   (crank the knob to make spin really matter)
```

Spin is now a **bounded pre-stress** instead of a giant energy term, so a default-spin asteroid fractures like a non-spinning one (energy-driven), and fast spin only *helps* a bit — exactly as physics should behave.

## What changed
- **Removed the additive `E_spin`** — impact energy is now just the bullet's `½mv²`. (This was the ~16-million-unit monster swamping everything.)
- The old **"Spin energy frac"** slider is now **"Spin pre-stress"** (default 0.12, range 0–2): it controls how strongly spin *weakens tangential bonds*, which is the real, bounded mechanism for "spinning rocks cleave into radial wedges." Setting it to 0 no longer kills all fracture — it just removes spin's contribution; the bullet still does its thing.

## This should also fix your directionality complaint (#2)
Directionality "only worked at max" because the spin energy made the budget **unlimited** — every bond broke regardless, so there was nothing to steer. Now that fractures are **budget-limited**, the propagation actually has to *choose* which bonds to spend on, and it spends forward-aligned ones first. So at a moderate directionality (0.4–0.6) you should now clearly see the crack drive along the shot. **Please re-test it** — if it still feels too subtle, I can sharpen the response curve, but I expect the budget fix resolves it.

## Updated starting point
Defaults now give ~5 chunks per hit. Your dials, in order of impact:
- **Fracture eff.** (0.12) — master "how shattery." 
- **Bullet speed/mass / Energy ×** — raw energy (speed is quadratic, so it's the strong one).
- **Toughness** (now live) — material hardness.
- **Spin pre-stress** — only matters for fast-spinning asteroids (raise it + asteroid spin to see radial cleaving).

Rebuild and give it a drive — between this and the contact-normal fix earlier, the destruction should finally feel controllable and physical. Tell me how directionality reads now.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 12:24 UTC

Both are real and related — and both come from the same scale mismatch we keep hitting.

**Issue 1:** the "momentum kick" is computed as physical momentum: `bulletMomentum / asteroidMass ≈ (0.2 × 900) / 43000 ≈ 0.004 px/s`. A light bullet against a heavy asteroid imparts *nothing* — physically correct, but dead for game feel. And the radial spread is derived from kinetic energy via `√(2E/m)`, which with those masses is also ~1 px/s. So the only velocity fragments actually get is the **spin carry-over** (`ω × r`), exactly as you saw.

**Issue 2:** because fragments have almost no outward velocity, they spawn touching/overlapping their siblings and just *sit there*, so the contact solver has to grind them apart over many frames → the violent "teleport out" with spurious energy/spin.

Fixing #1 (real ejection velocity) largely fixes #2 (they fly apart on their own), and I'll add a short **spawn-grace** so fresh fragments don't fight each other at all while separating.

Let me decouple fragment velocity from the tiny physical numbers and tie it to bullet speed (intuitive: faster bullet → fragments fly faster), with a directional forward push. Engine first:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-01 12:27 UTC

Both fixed, builds clean.

## Issue 1 — fragments now fly in the bullet direction
The momentum kick was computed as *physical* momentum (`bulletMomentum / asteroidMass`), which is ~0.004 px/s — invisible. I decoupled fragment dynamics from the (tiny, mass-mismatched) physical numbers and tied them to **bullet speed** instead, which is intuitive *and* visible:

- **Bullet push** (renamed from "Momentum xfer"): forward velocity *along the shot*, = `bulletSpeed × 0.15` ≈ **135 px/s** at default. This is the directional knock you were missing.
- **Eject speed** (new): radial scatter *away from the impact point*, = `bulletSpeed × 0.08`, with **smaller fragments flying faster**.
- Spin carry-over (`ω × r`) is still there.

So a hit now = forward push + outward scatter + inherited spin. Two new live sliders to tune the feel.

## Issue 2 — no more violent squeeze-out
The root cause was exactly your diagnosis: fragments spawned *touching* their siblings with ~0 velocity, so the contact solver had to grind them apart over many frames → the "teleport out + spurious energy/spin." Two changes fix it:
1. They now have **real ejection velocity** (issue 1), so they separate on their own.
2. A **0.3 s spawn-grace**: fresh fragments spawn on a non-colliding "Ghost" layer and drift apart freely; collision switches on only after they've cleared the overlap. So the solver never fights packed fragments.

One minor side effect of the grace: you can't shoot a fragment for the first 0.3 s after it spawns (it's intangible). That's usually imperceptible; if it bugs you I can shorten it.

Rebuild and give it a drive — shooting should now visibly *blast* chunks in the direction you fired, and a shatter should expand cleanly outward instead of churning. **Bullet push** and **Eject speed** are your new feel dials (and **Directionality** should read clearly now too).

All three addressed, plus two new sliders:

### 1 — Spin now follows the hit
Fragment spin was `parentSpin ± random`. It's now `parentSpin + ImpactSpin × cross(spreadDir, shotDir)`: fragments on the same side of the shot line shear the **same way** (consistent), forward fragments spin little, with just a touch of random variety. New **Impact spin** slider (default 4). *If the rotation sense feels backwards to you, say so and I'll flip the sign — `cross` direction is a convention.*

### 2 — Cells can now vaporize (your absorption idea)
The propagation already computes the crack energy *reaching each cell*. Cells whose absorbed energy crosses a threshold are now **pulverized → debris**, so the asteroid actually loses that material. New **Blast (vaporise)** slider:
- It's the energy threshold as a fraction of impact energy: **lower = bigger crater** (more cells vaporize), **0 = off**.
- Default **0.7** vaporizes just the struck cell — every hit now punches a little crater and the cell *disappears*. Drop it toward 0.3 for a proper blast that pulverizes a cluster.
- The surviving body is re-spawned **with those cells removed**, so repeated hits erode an asteroid away cell by cell.

(For *true* recursive sub-splitting — a cell breaking into smaller cells rather than dust — that's a heavier "Option C" feature; this vaporize-to-debris gives the visible result and the erosion you wanted.)

### 3 — Subsurface fragmentation fixed for free
Because the struck cell now vaporizes, the **surface opens** at the impact point, so interior fragments are no longer trapped under intact surface cells — the crater releases them. So directional shots now blow *through* the surface instead of leaving it intact with a hidden fracture underneath.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-02 08:07 UTC

Good list — this is the right moment to plan it, because some of these are quick tweaks, one is an architectural keystone, and one is a real fork in the road. Let me sort them by what they actually are, then propose an order.

## Quick read on the six

| # | Item | Type | Effort | Block the game? |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 3 | Bullet push too high | tuning | **S** (1 line) | no — do now |
| 4 | Parameter ownership (bullet vs material vs edge) | **architecture** | M | **yes** — defines the data model |
| 6 | Test materials (rock/glass…) | content/validation | S–M | depends on #4 |
| 2 | Render stuck cells as one | renderer | M | makes it *look* right |
| 1 | VFX (dust, obliteration, tracer) | renderer polish | M | no — additive |
| 5 | Multi-frame crack propagation | **engine rework** | L | **decide now**, build later |

## The keystone: #4 — who owns each parameter

This is the one to settle first, because it decides how weapons and materials are *defined* for the whole game. Right now everything is one flat blob of global tunables; you've correctly spotted it should split three ways:

**Material** — intrinsic to the rock (a per-body `FractureProperties` component; rock ≠ glass ≠ metal):
`Toughness · Brittleness · GrainArea · Density · MinFragmentArea · KineticFraction · SurfaceEfficiency · SpinPreStress`

**Weapon/projectile** — intrinsic to the bullet type (a `WeaponProfile`, passed per shot; AP round ≠ scattergun ≠ explosive):
`Speed · Mass · Directionality · Blast · BulletPush · EjectSpeed · ImpactSpin`

**Edge/bond** — advanced, optional: per-bond **strength multipliers** so an asteroid can have baked **fault lines** (weak cleavage planes). This resurrects the old "fault angles" idea, now as the natural per-bond property.

Concretely the refactor is small: `FractureService.TryFracture` *already* separates a `FractureSettings` (weapon-ish) from the body's `Material`. We just **move `SurfaceEfficiency` + `SpinPreStress` into `FractureProperties`** (they're material-dissipation properties, not weapon properties), **rename `FractureSettings` → `WeaponProfile`**, and split the demo's tuner into a **Material panel** and a **Weapon panel**. After that, "different bullets" and "test materials" both fall out naturally.

## The one real fork: #5 — stateful, multi-frame fracture

This isn't polish — it changes the fracture **API shape**. Today `TryFracture` runs the whole BFS synchronously and returns the result in one frame (stateless). Multi-frame means the body gets a **`FractureInProgress` component** holding a crack frontier that advances one BFS ring per fixed step; bonds break progressively; components+fragments are only computed when the front settles. That's a genuinely different, stateful model.

It's very cool (you'd *watch* the crack race through the rock) but it's an L-sized engine rework. My recommendation: **decide the approach now** (so we don't bake assumptions that block it), but **implement it as its own phase** — the game can ship on instant fracture and switch later, because the *inputs* (weapon + material + impact) don't change, only *when* the result arrives.

## Recommended order

1. **#3** bullet-push default — 1 line, right now.
2. **#4** the split (`WeaponProfile` + `Material`) — the keystone; unblocks the rest.
3. **#6** material presets + an in-demo selector — validates #4 and gives you rock/glass/metal to feel.
4. **#2** render-as-one (silhouette + visible cracks) — makes it look like rock, not a Voronoi diagram. *(My approach: fill cells with no internal strokes, and stroke only **boundary edges** — edges a cell doesn't share with a living neighbor — so the outline + any cracks show automatically.)*
5. **#1** VFX — dust bursts on pulverize, cell-obliteration flash, raycast bullet tracer.
6. **#5** multi-frame propagation — its own phase, after the above (or interleaved if you want it sooner).

## Where to start

My instinct: do **#3 + #4 together** (tiny tweak + the architecture), then **#2** so it *looks* right while we test materials in **#6**. VFX (#1) and the multi-frame rework (#5) come after.

Two questions before I touch code:
- For **#4**, are you happy with the material/weapon/edge split above — specifically moving `SurfaceEfficiency` and `SpinPreStress` to *material*, and keeping blast/push/eject/spin/directionality as *weapon*?
- For **#2 (render-as-one)**: do you want internal cell lines **fully hidden** (only silhouette + open cracks visible), or **faintly shown** (so you can still see the cell structure)? That changes the renderer approach.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-02 11:54 UTC

Good — keeping `MomentumTransfer` (I see you've already dropped it to 0.01), and **fully hidden** internal lines for render-as-one. Let me answer the multi-frame question, then start #4.

## Multi-frame fracture — what it implies

Today fracture is **synchronous and stateless**: `TryFracture` runs the whole BFS in one call and hands back the `FractureResult` immediately. Multi-frame makes it a **stateful process that lives on the body across frames** — the crack front advances one BFS ring per fixed step. Concretely:

**1. State moves onto the body.** The propagation locals (`energy[]`, `broken[]`, the frontier, the remaining budget, plus the impact params) become a component, e.g. `FractureProcess`, attached to the struck body. The body stays whole but carries an in-progress crack.

**2. A new engine system drives it.** `FractureStepSystem` runs each fixed step and advances every in-progress `FractureProcess` by one (or N) BFS rings — breaking a few bonds, pushing energy to the next cells. When a front settles (budget spent / no eligible bonds), it commits: connected-components → fragments.

**3. The API splits into begin + complete.**
- `BeginFracture(world, body, hit, weapon)` — threshold check; if it passes, attach `FractureProcess` (energy seeded at the struck cell) and return "started". Sub-threshold still accumulates instantly as now.
- Completion is **asynchronous**: the result arrives N frames later via a `FractureCompletedEvent { Entity, FractureResult }` the game subscribes to — instead of a synchronous return. This is actually the "thin engine + events" contract from the spec, finally realized.

**4. New behaviors to handle.**
- **Re-hit mid-fracture**: a second bullet deposits its energy into the *existing* process at its struck cell (the crack just gets more fuel) — natural and cool.
- **Crack speed**: rings-per-step becomes a tunable (a brittle material cracks faster).
- **Rendering**: with render-as-one, the broken bonds *are* the visible cracks — so you literally watch fractures race across the rock before it lets go. That's the payoff.

**5. What does *not* change.** The inputs — weapon + material + impact — are identical. Only *when* and *how* the result is delivered changes. So building the game on the synchronous version and swapping later is safe; the only game-side change is hit-handling shifts from "spawn fragments now" to "spawn fragments when the event fires."

**Cost:** statefulness + async results + the edge cases above. It's an L-sized, self-contained phase — which is why it's last. Now, #4:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-02 12:08 UTC

Builds clean — **#2 done**.

## Cells now render as one
- At spawn, each body precomputes its **boundary edges** (`RenderOutline` component): a cell edge whose midpoint isn't shared with another cell. That's the outer silhouette + any crater rims, computed once via a midpoint hash (cheap).
- The renderer **fills cells with no per-cell stroke** (so adjacent cells of the same color merge into one shape) and **strokes only the boundary**.

Result: an intact asteroid shows just its **outline**; vaporized cells leave **craters** that outline themselves automatically; and when it splits, each fragment is its own clean silhouette. The Voronoi-diagram look is gone.

Two honest caveats:
- **Faint fill seams**: adjacent filled cells *can* show a sub-pixel anti-aliasing seam where they meet. On the dark rock fill it's usually invisible — but if it bugs you, the bulletproof fix is a one-shot multi-contour fill (a small `IRenderer.FillPath` addition); I left that out to keep it simple. Tell me if you see seams.
- **Hairline cracks**: a crack that *doesn't* fully separate the body won't show, because those cells still physically touch (shared edge → hidden). Only **craters** (vaporized cells) and full **splits** are visible. True hairline cracks would need bond-based edge rendering (hide an edge only if the two cells are still *bonded*) — a worthwhile follow-up, but heavier; flagging it as optional.

Rebuild and take a look — it should finally read as solid rock that cracks apart, and cycling materials with **M** should now *look* distinct too (glass = many fine shards, metal = chunky).

Want me to go ahead with all three, or pick a subset?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-02 12:23 UTC

All build clean — **#2 fully done**, including both requests.

## Seams gone — `FillPath`
Added `IRenderer.FillPath(verts, contourLengths, color)` — fills **all of a body's cells as one path** in a single draw (nonzero winding), so the union rasterizes seamlessly. No more AA lines between cells; it's a solid shape. (Implemented in the Skia backend; the renderer builds the world-vert mesh into reused buffers, zero per-cell draw calls.)

## Hairline cracks
Two parts:
- **Engine:** a surviving body now **keeps only its *unbroken* bonds**. A bond that broke without disconnecting the body becomes a permanent gap in the bond graph — so the body is genuinely weakened there (progressive damage) *and* the crack is recorded.
- **Renderer:** `ComputeEdges` now classifies each shared edge using the bond graph — an internal edge is hidden **only if its two cells are still bonded**; if the bond is gone (but the cells still touch), it's drawn as a **crack** (a thin, darkened-fill line). Unshared edges remain the silhouette.

So now: an intact asteroid is a clean solid shape; a hit that doesn't fully shatter it leaves **visible fissures** that persist and weaken it; keep hitting and the cracks spread until it splits; vaporized cells still carve craters. That's the full "silhouette + cracks, internal lines hidden" you wanted.

Rebuild and shoot something gently a few times — you should see cracks web across it before it lets go.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 08:15 UTC

Good call to plan these — they're the difference between "physics demo" and "game." Here's my proposed design, then a few decisions to lock.

## Architecture: a particle system fed by physics

VFX are presentation, so they belong in the **game layer**, not the engine — but they should be *driven* by the physical data the engine already produces. The good news: **no engine changes are needed.** Everything the VFX want already exists:

- `FractureResult` → `EnergySurface`, `EnergyKinetic`, `ImpactPointWorld`, and per-fragment `Mass`/`Area`/`Linear`/`Angular` (including the pulverized debris specs).
- The hit → bullet velocity/direction, and the asteroid's material + `AsteroidColor`.

So I'd add a small **pooled particle system** (positions, velocities, drag, life, size start→end, color start→end) updated kinematically and drawn via `IRenderer`. Emitters read the physics and spawn particles. Pool-capped (~2000), recycle oldest.

## The three effects, as profiles

Each effect is a **profile** (designer params) that physical quantities **modulate** — that's the key pattern: tunable base values × physics.

**Dust** (on pulverized cells): `Count · Size(start→end) · Intensity(alpha) · Ttl · Speed · Spread(cone) · DirBias(forward↔radial) · Drag`.
→ modulated by: count ∝ blast energy & cell area · speed ∝ ejection · direction = blend(shot dir, radial-from-impact) · **color from the material** · size ∝ cell area.

**Impact flash** (at the hit): `Size(start→end) · Intensity · Ttl · Color(hot→fade) · Ring|Disk`.
→ modulated by: size & brightness ∝ impact energy · hotter color at higher energy · material tint.

**Bullet tracer**: `Length · Width · Color(core→fade) · Ttl`.
→ modulated by: length/brightness ∝ bullet speed · color from the weapon.

## On physical dependence — my opinion: do it, it's easy

It's both **very feasible and the thing that makes destruction VFX feel real**, and it costs almost nothing because the engine already hands you the numbers — the emitter just reads `EnergySurface`, a fragment's `Linear`/`Area`, the shot direction, the material color. The only real work is *tuning the curves* (energy→count, ejection→speed), not plumbing. So the profile = **base params + a couple of modulation coefficients**, and physics scales it per hit. A weak tap → a puff of slow dust + a dim flash; a hard shot → a cone of fast sparks + a bright flash. That asymmetry is exactly what sells it, and it falls out for free.

A few genuine forks before I build:

Summary:
1. Primary Request and Intent:
   The user is building a 2D game engine in C#/.NET 8 specialized in **physics-based destruction** of asteroids, for a game "Asteroids on Steroids" (single-player, wave-based, ~10× screen toroidal world). The destruction must be realistic/complex: rotation, fracturing based on bullet mass/speed/density/rotation/size, concave compound shapes. The engine must support different UIs (WinForms as a future backend). Over the conversation the intent evolved through: design review → prototyping → spec → full engine implementation (Phases A-D) → a live-tunable destruction sandbox demo → iterative bug-fixing and calibration based on the user playing the demo → a final planning session of 6 pre-game polish/architecture tasks being implemented in agreed order.

   The most recent explicit request: implement a planned list of 6 items "in that order": #3 bullet push (done), #4 parameter ownership split, #6 material presets, #2 render-cells-as-one (with internal cell lines **completely hidden**), #1 VFX, #5 multi-frame fracture propagation (last). User also asked me to explain what multi-frame fracture would imply for the API.

2. Key Technical Concepts:
   - **Cell/bond-graph fracture model**: asteroids pre-fractured into convex Voronoi cells joined by a bond graph; a fracture-energy budget breaks bonds; connected components = fragments. Concavity = absent cells.
   - **Energy budget propagation**: directionality REORDERS which bonds break (not how many); brittleness = transmission/reach; spin = bounded bond pre-stress (NOT additive energy).
   - ECS with sparse sets (World, Entity, ISystem, ForEach<T1,T2,T3>, GetComponent ref, HasComponent, IsAlive, CreateEntity, AddComponent, DestroyEntity, QueryEntities, Count, FlushDeferred).
   - Platform Abstraction Layer (PAL): IRenderer (immediate-mode), Color, FontSpec, IResourceManager, IAudioBackend, IGameWindow.
   - Fixed timestep + interpolation (FixedTimestep, Transform.PreviousPosition/PreviousRotation, PreviousStateSystem, teleport guard).
   - Raycast bullets (PhysicsQueries.Raycast, RayCastResult with PartIndex, Cyrus-Beck ray-vs-convex, quadratic ray-vs-circle).
   - Contact solver: iterative sequential impulses + Coulomb friction + sleeping; contact manifolds for compounds; soft positional correction (Baumgarte).
   - CompoundShape internal broad-phase (local-AABB cull, O(1) GetAABB).
   - Material (FractureProperties) vs Weapon (WeaponProfile) parameter ownership.
   - Render-as-one via boundary-edge silhouette (cell edges not shared with a neighbor).

3. Files and Code Sections:
   - **GameEngine/AsteroidsEngine.csproj**: UI-free Library, excludes Demos/**, Platform/**, Engine/Rendering/GameWindow.cs.

   - **GameEngine/Platform/Sdl/SkiaRenderer.cs**: SkiaSharp IRenderer impl.
   - **GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs**: contact manifold + iterative solver. Critical normal-orientation fix:
     ```
     Vector2 ab = tA.Position - tB.Position;
     for (int k = 0; k < _hitBuf.Count; k++)
         if (Vector2.Dot(_hitBuf[k].Normal, ab) < 0f)
             _hitBuf[k] = _hitBuf[k].Flipped();
     ```
     `CollectPairContacts` (compound → manifold via `CompoundShape.CollectContacts`); soft `TrySeparate` (`MathF.Max(0f, depth - PenetrationSlop) * CorrectionPercent`, slop 0.5, percent 0.4).
   - **GameEngine/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs**: `CollectContacts(...)` returns one contact per overlapping cell; internal broad-phase via precomputed `_localAabbs`/`_localBounds`.

Energy = eImpact only (no E_spin). kick = dir * (bulletSpeed * weapon.MomentumTransfer); ejectSpeed = bulletSpeed * weapon.EjectFraction.
   - **GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs**: Simulate reads surfEff from `mat.SurfaceEfficiency`, spin from `mat.SpinPreStress`. Propagate fills `energy[]` (now a param). Pulverization: `if (input.BlastFraction > 0f) { float blastThresh = input.BlastFraction * input.EnergyTotal; cells with energy >= blastThresh → pulverized }`. ConnectedComponents(n, bonds, broken, pulverized, out count) — pulverized get comp=-1. BuildFragments emits component fragments + pulverized debris. `FragmentMotion(...)` computes linear (parentLinear + rotVel + MomentumKick + spread*spreadSpeed) and angular (parentAngular + ImpactSpin * cross(spreadDir, ImpactDir) + small random). debris fly 1.6× faster.

     public void CycleMaterial() { _matIndex = (_matIndex+1)%Presets.Length; var m = Presets[_matIndex].Mat; Brittleness.Value = m.Brittleness; ... Density.Value = m.Density; }

Newly added `ComputeOutline(Cell[] cells)`:
     ```
     private static Vector2[] ComputeOutline(Cell[] cells) {
         var count = new Dictionary<(int,int),int>();
         foreach cell, foreach edge: mid=(v[i]+v[(i+1)%n])*0.5f; key=((int)Round(mid.X*2),(int)Round(mid.Y*2)); count[key]++;
         var segs = new List<Vector2>();
         foreach cell, foreach edge: if count[key]==1 { segs.Add(a); segs.Add(b); }
         return segs.ToArray();
     }
     ```
     Renderer asteroid loop now: `ForEach<Transform, FracturableBody, AsteroidColor>((Entity e, ...) => { fill cells with col.Fill (no DrawPolygon stroke); if HasComponent<RenderOutline>(e) draw boundary segments transformed by pose with col.Outline, 1.5f })`. HUD shows fps/asteroids/material; controls hint includes "M material".

   - **prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html**: validated design reference.

4. Errors and fixes:
   - **Collision: fragments absorb + spin uncontrollably**: Root cause was FLIPPED SAT normal — polygon-polygon SAT returns normal A→B but CollisionSystem/TrySeparate expects B→A. First attempt (contact manifolds + soft correction) didn't fix it. Real fix: orient every contact normal by relative position `if (Dot(normal, posA-posB) < 0) flip`. User confirmed it didn't work the first attempt, leading to the real diagnosis.
   - **Always full-shatter even at min bullet/max toughness**: Root cause (user diagnosed) was additive E_spin = SpinEnergyFraction × ½Iω² ≈ 16 million dwarfing bullet energy ~81000. Fix: removed additive E_spin; spin became bounded pre-stress. Earlier partial fix added SurfaceEfficiency coefficient (0.12). User: "The main one is spin energy frac, if I make it = 0, practically no hit has any force and gets completely absorbed."
   - **Fragments don't move in bullet direction; only spin matters**: momentum kick = bulletMomentum/asteroidMass ≈ 0.004 px/s (negligible). Fix: tie fragment velocity to bullet SPEED via MomentumKick = dir × bulletSpeed × MomentumTransfer and EjectSpeed = bulletSpeed × EjectFraction.
   - **Fragments trapped inside teleport out generating energy/spin**: fixed by real ejection velocity + spawn-grace ghost layer (user later set grace to 0.0f, effectively disabling it).
   - **Spin sometimes opposite to hit (random)**: angular was parentAngular + random(±). Fix: impact-induced shear spin = ImpactSpin × cross(spreadDir, shotDir). Noted: "If the rotation sense feels backwards, flip the sign."
   - **Build errors during edits**: `eff` name collision in FractureSimulator (renamed my local to surfEff); `input` captured in local function (copied to bodyPos local); FractureService.cs reformatted by linter (single-space) causing one Edit mismatch (re-read and matched actual text); Tuner.cs needed `using AsteroidsEngine.Engine.Components;` for FractureProperties.
   - **Spurious OmniSharp diagnostics**: collection-expression `[` warnings from misc-files fallback — ignored; dotnet build is source of truth.

## Engine — reusable `ParticleSystem`
[Engine/Effects/ParticleSystem.cs](GameEngine/Engine/Effects/ParticleSystem.cs) — a fixed-capacity pool (default 4096) of kinematic particles: position/velocity/drag, life, and **size + color that interpolate from birth→death**. `Emit` / `Update(dt)` / `Draw(IRenderer)`, swap-remove, zero per-frame allocation. Game-agnostic — it draws through the PAL, so any future game (or the WinForms backend) reuses it. Added `Color.Lerp` alongside.

## The three effects (demo-side, physics-driven)
- **Dust** ([`EmitDust`](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs)): each vaporized cell sprays a burst — **count ∝ impact energy**, **direction & speed from the cell's own ejection velocity** (+ a tunable cone), **size ∝ cell area**, **color from the material**, fading to transparent with drag. This replaced the old single-dot debris entirely.
- **Impact flash** ([`EmitFlash`](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs)): a hot disk at the hit point that expands and fades; **size ∝ impact energy**, so a tap is a dim spark and a heavy shot is a bright pop.
- **Tracer** = streak **+** sparks (as you picked): the streak is a tapering glow+hot-core line behind each round (derived from its velocity, length/width tunable); the sparks are short-lived hot flecks shed along the path each step (emitted in [RaycastBulletSystem](GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs)).

## Live tuning
New **`-- VFX --`** group in the panel (Tab): `Dust count/size/ttl/speed/spread`, `Flash size/ttl`, `Tracer length/width`. The physical modulation (energy→count/flash-size, ejection→dust direction/speed, area→dust size, material→color) is automatic on top of these base values. Panel still fits in the 800px window.

The energy modulation is anchored to a default shot (`EnergyRef = 80000`) so a stock bullet reads as "1×" and the sliders feel centered.

Rebuild and shoot — you should get tracer rounds streaking out, a flash on contact, and rock spraying material-colored dust where cells vaporize, all scaling with how hard you hit. Tune the VFX group to taste; tell me what feels off and I'll adjust the curves. Once you're happy, the last item is **#5 — multi-frame crack propagation**.

I think blast value is overtuned or doesn't work as intended: even at blast = 0.05 cells hardly ever separate and instead every cell impacted gets obliterated.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 13:05 UTC

Let me look at how blast actually drives pulverization in the simulator:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 13:05 UTC

Now let me read `Propagate` to see the scale of `energy[]` relative to `EnergyTotal`:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 13:07 UTC

Found it — and it's exactly inverted. The struck cell's energy is pinned to the **full** `EnergyTotal` ([FractureSimulator.cs:147](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs#L147)), while the pulverize threshold is `BlastFraction × EnergyTotal` ([line 74](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs#L74)). So:

- The struck cell vaporizes whenever `EnergyTotal ≥ BlastFraction × EnergyTotal`, i.e. for **any blast ≤ 1** — the cell you hit is *always* obliterated.
- **Lower** blast → **lower** threshold → **more** cells vaporize. So blast=0.05 makes a threshold of 5% of impact energy → nearly everything the crack touched turns to dust, and there's nothing left to separate into fragments. The slider runs backwards.

The fix: invert it so the threshold is `(1 − blast) × EnergyTotal` with a strict `>`. Now blast is "crater size": **0 = nothing vaporizes (pure fragmentation), 1 = everything the crack reached**, and because energy decays outward from the struck cell, intermediate values carve a crater of just the hottest cells around the hit.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 13:07 UTC

Fixed and building clean. The blast slider now reads the way you'd expect:

- **blast = 0** → nothing vaporizes; the asteroid purely **fragments/separates** (and cracks).
- **blast = 0.3** (new default) → a **small crater**: only the hottest cells right around the hit vaporize, the rest break off as fragments.
- **blast → 1** → everything the crack reached turns to dust (total disintegration).

The two changes that mattered: the threshold is now `(1 − blast) × impact energy` instead of `blast × impact energy` (so raising blast *grows* the crater instead of shrinking it), and it uses a strict `>` so that at blast = 0 even the struck cell — which holds exactly the full impact energy — survives. I also clamped blast to [0, 1], retuned the slider to 0–1, and set the weapon default to 0.3.

So your old symptom (blast = 0.05 obliterating everything) becomes blast = 0.05 → only the single struck cell vaporizes, everything else separates. Rebuild and you should be able to dial the crater from "clean break" up to "vaporize it all."

One note while you're tuning: blast and **Min frag area** interact — even with blast = 0, cells below min-frag-area still come off as debris (dust now), so if you want truly zero dust, drop min-frag-area too. Want me to hold here for you to test, or move on to **#5 (multi-frame crack propagation)**?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 15:12 UTC

Good — this is the most architecturally interesting of the bunch, so let me lay out the design and surface the real decisions. The nice part: we **already built crack rendering** (broken-but-attached bonds draw as fissures), so a multi-frame process literally animates cracks webbing across the rock — that visualization comes for free.

## The core shift in model

Today: hit → `Simulate` runs the whole solve atomically → original entity destroyed, fragments spawned, same frame.

Multi-frame: the asteroid **stays one live entity** and cracks spread through its bond graph over time. A chunk becomes its own entity only at the instant it fully disconnects. So: hit seeds a crack **front** → over frames the front breaks bonds (and vaporizes cells, emitting dust as it goes) → whenever the bond graph splits, the detached component flies off → when the front runs out of energy, the body is left permanently cracked (weaker for the next hit). The rigid body keeps moving/colliding as one piece the whole time.

## Component/system pair (current code untouched)

- New **`FractureProcess`** component on the cracking body: the live solver state — active fronts (each with its own energy field, impact direction, remaining budget), the body's accumulating broken/pulverized masks, a kinetic snapshot (kick / eject / impact-spin for when pieces detach), and the timing counters.
- New **`FractureCrackSystem`** (fixed-step): advances every `FractureProcess`, detaches fragments, emits events, finalizes.
- New entry **`FractureService.BeginFracture(...)`**: first hit attaches a `FractureProcess`; later hits push another front onto it. The existing `TryFracture` / `Simulate` / `FractureSimulator` stay exactly as-is — I'd only factor the *single frontier-pop* into a shared kernel both call, so the physics has one source of truth and your old path is preserved for fallback.

## Your two timing knobs

`StepsPerIteration` (frontier-pops per iteration) and `FramesPerIteration` (fixed-steps to wait between iterations). Together they set crack velocity = `StepsPerIteration / (FramesPerIteration · dt)` cells/sec — so you can go chunky (many steps, rarely) or smooth-slow (1 step every few frames). Both exposed live.

## API shift (worth being explicit)

Because the solve now spans frames, `BeginFracture` is **fire-and-forget**. The system raises `FragmentDetachedEvent` as pieces separate and `FractureCompletedEvent` at the end. The demo's current "spawn fragments from the returned result" block moves into those handlers. Detached chunks default to **frozen** (done cracking, but re-fracturable by a fresh hit); the leftover energy stays with the main body's fronts.

Now the three decisions I want your call on — my recommendation is the first option in each:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 15:18 UTC

All three locked to the recommended path. Before I write a large pile of code, here's the concrete blueprint so you can course-correct early — then I'll start on the engine core.

## Data + flow

**`CrackFront`** (engine) — one hit's live crack field: its own `Energy[]` per cell, `Processed[]`, a `Frontier` list, remaining `Budget`, `ImpactDirLocal`, `Directionality`, `Transmission`, and a precomputed `BlastThresh`. `Active` = budget left and frontier non-empty.

**`FractureProcess`** (engine component, on the live body) — `List<CrackFront> Fronts`, the accumulating `Broken[]`/`Pulverized[]` masks, the `Eff[]` bond-strength snapshot, the adjacency cache, the kinetic snapshot (kick / eject / impact-spin / dir for fling), and the timing (`StepsPerIteration`, `FramesPerIteration`, `FrameCounter`).

**Shared kernel** — I factor the inner frontier-pop out of `Propagate` into `FractureKernel.StepFront(front, cells, bonds, adj, eff, broken)` = exactly one pop. The existing atomic `Propagate` becomes `while (front.Active) StepFront(...)` — **behavior identical**, so your current path is untouched, just sharing the physics kernel.

**`FractureCrackSystem`** (fixed-step):
1. `FrameCounter++`; if `< FramesPerIteration`, skip.
2. Otherwise run `StepsPerIteration` pops across the body's active fronts (co-propagating through the **shared** `Broken[]`).
3. Scan front energies → newly crossed cells get `Pulverized` + a `CellPulverizedEvent` (→ dust).
4. When **all** fronts go inactive → finalize: run the existing `BuildFragments` on the final masks, emit a `FragmentDetachedEvent` per piece + `FractureCompletedEvent`, destroy the original. The demo spawns from those events.

**Entry:** `FractureService.BeginFracture(...)` — first hit attaches a `FractureProcess` and seeds a front; a later hit on a body that already has one just **pushes another front** (your opposite-direction case co-propagates and they fuse where they meet).

## One scope call I'm making (tell me if you disagree)

**v1 finalizes the whole body when all fronts exhaust** — i.e. cracks spread live and you watch them web across, then it breaks into pieces; I'm *not* doing mid-propagation "a chunk falls off while the rest is still cracking." Reason: a front exhausts its budget within a fraction of a second, so the only time this is visible is a second hit landing *inside that sub-second window* — and there, holding the body together until both fronts finish is exactly the co-propagation you asked for. Incremental detachment needs live parent-rebuild + front re-indexing for a case that's nearly invisible, so I'd defer it to v1.1. The end-state is identical to your trusted atomic fracture, just time-released.

## Speed ownership

Material gets a `CrackSpeed` (brittle→fast, tough→slow) that sets the default `StepsPerIteration`; the demo exposes `StepsPerIteration` + `FramesPerIteration` as live global overrides. Live cracks render by recomputing edges from the current masks each frame (cheap, only active bodies).

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-03 15:35 UTC

All six pre-game items are done. **Multi-frame crack propagation** is fully implemented and building clean across engine + demo.

## What you'll see
Shoot an asteroid → flash at impact → a **crack front spreads through the bond graph over the next frames** (you watch fissures web across the solid rock, dust puffing as cells vaporize) → when the front spends its energy, the body **breaks into the fragments**. The end-state is identical to the old instant fracture — just time-released.

## How it's built (engine, reusable)
- **`FractureKernel.StepFront` + `CrackFront`** — one frontier-pop factored out of the old `Propagate`. The atomic path now calls it in a loop, so single- and multi-frame fracture share *identical physics*. Your old `TryFracture`/`Simulate` path is untouched as a fallback.
- **`FractureProcess`** component + **`FractureCrackSystem`** — advances each live body's fronts `StepsPerIteration` pops every `FramesPerIteration` frames, vaporizes cells past the blast threshold, finalizes when all fronts die.
- **`FractureService.BeginFracture`** — fire-and-forget. First hit attaches a process; **a later hit pushes another co-propagating front** sharing the broken-bond state, so your opposite-direction case spreads from the far side and the two crack networks fuse where they meet.
- Async results: `CellPulverizedEvent` (→ live dust) and `FractureCompletedEvent` (→ demo spawns fragments, destroys the original).

## How to test the *feel*
The **`-- FRACTURE --`** panel group:
- **Crack steps/it** — material-owned (cycling materials with **M** fills it: glass = 6 → races, metal = 1 → slow tear). 
- **Frames/iter** — global override.

Default rock (2 steps / 1 frame at 120 Hz) cracks fast. **To really watch it crawl, bump Frames/iter up to ~8–12** and/or drop Crack steps/it to 1. Fire a second shot from another angle while it's mid-crack to see the fronts fuse.

## The one scope limit (as flagged)
A piece detaches only when **all** fronts finish — there's no "a chunk falls off while the rest is still cracking." It's invisible unless a second hit lands inside that sub-second window, where holding it together until both fronts finish *is* the co-propagation you wanted. Mid-spread detachment (live parent-rebuild) is the v1.1 if it ever feels needed.

I can't launch the window from here, so give it a run. If the crack pacing or the material speeds feel off, tell me what and I'll retune — otherwise Phase E is the actual game (waves, enemies, score, states, audio).

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-04 08:37 UTC

I think chunks should fall off as the cracks spread, not just at the end. What would it imply and what complications would there be? Why did you decide to not do it right away?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 08:39 UTC

Good push — and you're right to. Let me be straight about it, including where my earlier reasoning was weak.

## What it implies
Right now the body's cell/bond arrays are frozen for the whole process and only the *masks* grow. Mid-spread detachment means the live body has to actually **shrink** while it's still cracking: each iteration you run connected-components over the current broken/pulverized state, and any component that has **settled** (no active front still working inside it) gets cut loose as its own entity, while the piece(s) still being cracked carry on. So "detach" stops being a one-shot finalize and becomes a per-iteration check.

## The complications, concretely

1. **The index remap.** A `CrackFront` indexes `Energy[]`/`Processed[]`/`Frontier` by cell id; `Broken[]`/`Eff[]`/`Adj` by bond/cell id. The moment cells leave, every surviving index shifts, so all of that live solver state has to be remapped through an old→new index map. It's mechanical, but it's exactly the bookkeeping the finalize-at-end version never has to do.

2. **It bends the load-bearing invariant.** The whole engine rests on *"Transform.Position IS the world centroid, cells stored centroid-relative."* When a chunk leaves asymmetrically, the remainder's true centroid moves — so you either **recenter** (shift Position + rewrite local coords, which forces the remap) or carry a **running CoM offset** threaded through fill, outline, collision shape, and the ω×r fling math. Collision, inertia, and raycast all depend on that invariant, so this is the part that can destabilize things that currently work.

3. **Shape/mass/inertia churn.** Each detach recomputes the remainder's mass, inertia, and `CompoundShape` (AABBs, local bounds). Fine because splits are rare topological events — but a shot that sheds several chunks over a few frames rebuilds the body several times.

4. **Orphaned fronts.** A front's frontier can straddle a forming crack. When one side leaves, that side's in-flight energy is orphaned. Since we agreed detached chunks are **frozen** (don't keep cracking), you just drop it — acceptable, but it means a chunk can fly off marginally "less cracked" than if it had stayed.

5. **Velocity attribution.** Finalize flings everything from one snapshot. Mid-spread, the *correct* impulse for a chunk is whichever front cut it loose — so to be faithful you start attributing detachments to fronts. Skippable at first (use the latest snapshot), but it's a loose end.

6. **The multi-active-split edge.** If a split leaves active fronts in two separate components at once, one `FractureProcess` can't drive two entities. Either split the process across both (recursive) or just finalize early there. Rare; I'd finalize early.

## Why I deferred it — honestly
Three reasons, and one of them was partly weak:
- **Cost/benefit for a testable first cut.** Finalize-at-end gives you the live crack spread, dust, and co-propagating hits — the 80% — with none of the remap/invariant risk, and its end-state is *provably identical* to your trusted atomic fracture. Right thing to ship first.
- **Invariant risk.** This is the one feature that forces the centroid invariant to bend, and I didn't want to destabilize collision/inertia until you'd confirmed the *pacing* felt right. If the timing were wrong, the detachment work would've been wasted on the wrong foundation.
- **The weak part:** I justified it with "only visible in a sub-second window." That's true at the *fast default* pacing — but the whole point of the FRACTURE knobs is to make cracks crawl, and **at slow pacing a chunk visibly separates well before the front finishes elsewhere.** So for the use case you actually asked for, the limitation is genuinely noticeable, not an edge case. You're correct to want it.

## How I'd do it
In-place rebuild + remap (not new-entity), so the asteroid keeps its identity and the invariant stays clean: each iteration, detach every settled component via the existing `BuildFragments` (reused for one component → `FragmentDetachedEvent`), rebuild the remaining body's cells/bonds/shape/mass **in place** and recenter, then remap the surviving fronts through the index map. Frozen fragments, drop orphaned front energy, finalize-early on the rare multi-active split.

Want me to build that? It's the expensive 20%, but it's well-bounded and it's the thing that makes slow cracks actually shed rock the way you're picturing.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 09:14 UTC

Great questions — the second one actually lands on a better design than the one I proposed. Let me take them in order and then give you the honest verdict.

## Where the energy lives, and what happens to it

There are **two separate energy channels** in this model, and conflating them is the trap:

- **Surface (crack) energy** — the `CrackFront.Energy[]` field + remaining `Budget`. This is what propagates and breaks bonds.
- **Kinetic (fling) energy** — `eKinetic`, a separate share computed at the *threshold split* and turned into velocity by `FragmentMotion` (eject speed, kick, shear spin).

Fling velocity does **not** come from the residual crack field — it's the other channel. So in my "frozen + drop orphaned energy" sketch, the leftover crack energy in a detaching chunk is **discarded** (physically: "it radiated away as heat/sound and the chunk stopped cracking"). It is *not* transformed into velocity — doing that would be an ad-hoc hack that makes a fragment's speed depend on how *unfinished* its crack was, which is nonsense. That discard is exactly the source of the "behaves differently than if it stayed" artifact.

## Your idea is the right one — and it dissolves the worst complication

Spawning each split-off component as its own entity with its **own front carrying the energy still in its cells** is better than my version, and here's the part that matters: it **sidesteps the centroid-invariant problem I was worried about.**

My "shrink the body in place" approach is what forces the CoM drift / recenter-with-remap mess (complication #2 last message). But if every split instead **builds fresh entities for *all* resulting components** — exactly what the atomic `BuildFragments` already does, recentering and recomputing mass/inertia/shape correctly — then no body is ever mutated in place, the invariant holds automatically, and the *only* new work is **transferring the live front state onto the fresh bodies**:

- `Energy[]`, `Processed[]`, `Frontier`, `Broken[]` are all per-cell/per-bond → they **partition by membership for free** (each component keeps its own cells' values, remapped to compact indices).
- The one thing without a natural per-cell home is the scalar **`Budget`** — if a front straddles the split, you divide its remaining budget across the sides (proportional to each side's frontier energy is the defensible rule).

So a detached chunk keeps cracking *identically* to if it had stayed. No discarded energy, no behavioral seam. Conceptually it's even cleaner: there's no privileged "main body vs chunk" — **a split just partitions the process.**

## So is the blocker just rebuild cost? No — and that's the key correction

**Runtime cost is negligible.** Connected-components on 30–60 cells is microseconds, and you only rebuild *on a split event*, not every iteration — between splits the fronts just step in place. A body that splits 3 times rebuilds 3 times total. That's nothing.

The real costs are **complexity and a couple of genuine physics wrinkles**, not CPU:

1. **Front-partition + budget-split code.** Fiddly, conservation-sensitive (no lost/duplicated cells, energy adds up), needs real tests. This is the bulk of the work.
2. **Momentum jitter across repeated splits** — the subtle one. `FragmentMotion` is a *scatter* model, not strict momentum conservation. If you re-fling every component at every split, the surviving big piece gets nudged on each split and can visibly jitter under sustained fire. Mitigation: only fling the *smaller* separated pieces; let the largest continue with the parent's velocity. Doable, but it's feel-tuning, and it's a new distinction the current build doesn't make.
3. **Entity identity churn** — the continuing body becomes a new entity on each split. For asteroids that's fine (fragments are all new asteroids anyway); it'd only bite if something needed to track "the same asteroid" across a split (homing missile, health bar). Worth knowing before it surprises you later.

## Honest opinion: should you invest now?

**On the design:** if you build mid-spread detachment at all, build *your* version (partition into fresh sub-processes), not my frozen-drop one. Yours is more correct *and* avoids the invariant bending. I was wrong to lead with the in-place idea.

**On the timing — my actual reservation:** this is the deepest, most speculative fidelity feature in the destruction core, and the **game it serves doesn't exist yet** — no enemies, waves, scoring, or states. There's a real risk of polishing the fracture engine to a mirror finish while the thing it's for is unbuilt, then discovering in real play that asteroids shatter fast (action pacing, fast bullets) and this path rarely triggers — or that you want something else entirely. The multi-frame system is *already* mostly a visual flourish; mid-spread detachment is polish on polish, and it permanently grows the most intricate subsystem's surface area (every future energy-model change now has to respect front-partitioning).

**The cost in concrete terms:** roughly a focused day including tests, concentrated in the riskiest code, plus a new tunable (budget split) and a feel-tune (momentum jitter) to babysit. Not huge — but not free, and front-loaded onto fidelity rather than gameplay.

**My recommendation, framed as the decision you actually face:** is *slow, progressive, chunk-shedding fracture a signature pillar of this game* — big asteroids you visibly carve apart under sustained fire, a look players come for — or is it a sandbox curiosity you enjoy tuning? 
- If it's a **pillar**, build it now, do it your way; it's worth it and the foundation (kernel/front/process split) is already the right shape for it — it's purely additive, no rework.
- If it's a **curiosity**, ship the game on finalize-at-end (which is correct and genuinely good-looking), and add this later *exactly when real play demands it* — the architecture loses nothing by waiting.

I lean toward **defer and go build Phase E**, not because the feature is bad — your design is the right one — but because its value is unknowable until the game around it is real, and it's the kind of work that's much easier to justify (and tune) when you can feel it in actual combat. What's your read — pillar or curiosity?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 10:02 UTC

The per-cell normals come from `_parts[i].Intersects(...)` (SAT). The CollisionSystem comment admits the SAT normal sign is unreliable, which is *why* it falls back to the whole-body centroid heuristic — fine for convex, wrong for concave. Let me see how the SAT orients its normal:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 10:02 UTC

Let me read the polygon SAT and dispatch precisely:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 10:05 UTC

That's the whole story. The per-cell SAT (`SatPolygonPolygon`) actually orients its normal correctly **using the two contacting polygons' centroids** — but the dispatch swaps operands, so `CollectPairContacts` and then the `CollisionSystem` "fix" it with the **whole-body** centroid (`tA.Position - tB.Position`). For a body in a crater that whole-body vector points into the rock, so crater-wall normals get flipped inward → the body is shoved through.

The fix: orient each contact normal by the **contacting cell's** geometry (local), and delete the global whole-body flip.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 10:06 UTC

Now the CollisionSystem — flip per the new cell-oriented convention, and remove the global whole-body re-orientation:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 10:07 UTC

Both build clean. The fix is also regression-safe for the cases that already worked:

- **Convex–convex** now orients by `pa - pb`, which is *identically* the old whole-body test (`Dot(normal, tA−tB)`), so those contacts are unchanged.
- **External surface hits** (ship/chunk hitting an asteroid from outside): the contacting cell faces outward and the body is outside it, so cell-based and body-based orientation agree — also unchanged.
- **Only the crater/concave case changes**: the normal now points out of the *wall you're actually touching* instead of being flipped toward the far side.

I traced the sign carefully (ship outside → still pushed outward; ship in crater → now pushed out through the opening instead of into the rock), and the deepest-contact positional correction follows the same corrected normals.

## What to verify in the GUI
- Fly the ship into a deep crater / concave notch — it should slide along the walls and push *out*, no more snap-through or velocity spikes.
- Shoot a crater deep enough that a fragment spawns inside it — it should settle/eject naturally instead of teleporting through the asteroid.
- Normal stuff (bouncing off the outside, fragment-on-fragment resting) should feel exactly as before.

I can't drive the window from here, so give those a try. One caveat worth knowing: I used each cell's **AABB center** as the orientation reference (cheap, and we already cache it) — for the compact Voronoi cells that's effectively the centroid, but a pathologically elongated cell could in theory mis-sign; if you ever see a weird single-cell push, that's the suspect and I'd switch to the true polygon centroid.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 12:49 UTC

## What it does

Each iteration, if the body has broken into **≥2 components**, it splits *right then* into fresh re-centred bodies:
- The piece holding the **most live crack energy** becomes the **continuer** — it keeps the parent's motion (no fling, so no jitter) and **keeps cracking**: it carries a remapped `FractureProcess` whose fronts retain their `Energy[]`, `Processed[]`, frontier, and a proportional share of the remaining `Budget`. So it propagates exactly as if it had never detached.
- The other pieces **fling off** (settled ones as plain fragments/debris).
- It recurses naturally: a continuing piece that splits again just emits another split.

**Your energy question, answered in code:** the leftover crack energy in a piece is **carried, not discarded and not turned into velocity** — it stays in that piece's front and keeps breaking its bonds. Fling velocity remains the separate kinetic channel. So there's no behavioral seam between "detached" and "stayed attached."

## Toggle + fallback
New **`Detach split`** in the FRACTURE panel group: **1 = chunks peel off as cracks reach them** (default), **0 = the old finalize-at-end** (whole body splits at once). The old path is completely untouched — it's the exact same `BuildResult` call as before.

## Engine shape
- `BuildComponentSpec` (one component → re-centred body + index remap) is now shared by the atomic `BuildFragments` **and** the split — one source of truth.
- New `CountComponents` (cheap per-iteration split check), `SplitLive` + `PartitionFront`, `LivePiece`, and `FractureSplitEvent`. The kernel and atomic fracture are unchanged.

## What to test
- **Detach=1, slow pacing** (Frames/iter ≈ 8–12): shoot a big asteroid and watch chunks peel away *as the crack front reaches them* while the main mass keeps fracturing — then flip **Detach=0** and see the difference (cracks spread, then it all lets go at once).
- Things to watch for (this is the riskiest code and I can't see the window): **conservation** — no chunks vanishing or duplicating, no sudden velocity/spin spikes at a split. If you see any, that's a remap/partition bug and I'll add a headless conservation test to pin it.
- One known, benign quirk I flagged earlier: the continuing mass becomes a *new entity* on each split (fine for asteroids; only matters if something needs to track "the same rock" across a split).

Give the crater fix and this a run together and tell me how they feel.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-04 13:16 UTC

All three fixes are in and building clean.

## 1. Impact spin
Default dropped **4 → 0.5**, and the slider is now finer (range 0–10, step 0.1) so you can go lower. Cycling materials still fills the other sliders; this one's weapon-side so it stays where you set it.

## 2. Velocity timing (your issue #3)
- **Recoil now happens at impact.** The momentum kick (bullet push) is applied to the body's velocity the moment it's hit, in `BeginFracture` — the asteroid reacts immediately instead of waiting for the fracture to finish.
- **No more double-count / end-of-fracture lurch.** The kick was removed from the per-fragment fling; fragments inherit it through the body's velocity instead.
- **The surviving mass no longer gets a fresh fling.** In detach mode, by the time the body finalizes it's a single piece that already shed its chunks — it now just keeps its current motion (`fling: false`). The "too much velocity on the survivor" was exactly that end-of-fracture scatter impulse. (Finalize-at-end mode still bursts, as it should.)
- The **progressive** part you wanted is already there in detach mode: pieces fly off with their ejection as they detach, not all at the end.

## 3. Collision robustness (your issue #2)
Two safeguards in the contact solver, both targeting deep/sudden overlap:
- **Positional correction capped at 8 px/frame** — a body that finds itself deep inside another (internal fragment, warp wrap, respawn) now *oozes* out over a few frames instead of teleporting, and it actually resolves instead of sticking.
- **No restitution on deep overlaps** — an already-embedded body separates gently instead of bouncing/launching, which kills the "fragment teleports out and kicks the asteroid" explosion.

Combined with the earlier cell-oriented normal fix, internal fragments and warp/respawn overlaps should behave much better.

## Worth knowing
- A *perfectly symmetric* embedding (a body exactly surrounded on all sides) can still resolve slowly since the net push is near zero — rare, and it'll un-stick as soon as anything breaks the symmetry.
- **Respawn can still place asteroids overlapping** — they'll now ooze apart instead of sticking, but if you'd rather they never start overlapping I can add spawn-spacing (reject positions that overlap an existing body). Say the word.

Give it a run — the hit feedback should feel immediate now, the survivor shouldn't lurch, and craters/warps shouldn't fling things through the rock.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 08:24 UTC

**On your restitution question:** no, it uses entirely current-frame data. The contact normal comes from SAT at current positions, and the bias is `−e · vn` where `vn` is the current relative velocity at the contact point — no previous positions involved. `PreviousPosition` is only used by the interpolated renderer. So spawning-inside and warp overlaps have no previous-position to bounce off correctly — they just find themselves already penetrating and the solver tries to recover.

The shrink idea is the right call — it's a targeted solution to the exact root cause (the detached cell's collision shape is flush with the hole it came from, so it's immediately in deep contact with the walls). Let me implement it as a tunable parameter and also look at why the stuck issue persists:

Clean. Three changes:

## Detach shrink
Each detached fragment's cells are contracted toward their own centroids by `DetachCellScale` (default **0.90** = 10% smaller). The continuer keeps scale 1.0 since it stays in place. Tunable live as **Detach shrink** in the FRACTURE panel — slide toward 1.0 if it looks visually wrong, toward 0.7 if fragments still clip. The shrink applies uniformly per-cell so the crack-line geometry looks right (cells already separated by broken bonds won't suddenly gap further).

## Stuck entities
Two-part fix:
- **Velocity solve skipped for deep overlaps** — when `deepest.Depth >= DeepNoBounce` (6 px) we skip `GatherContact` entirely. The body still gets the capped positional push from `TrySeparate` each frame, but no velocity impulse that could flip direction or spin it up. So it oozes out without bouncing or locking.
- **Warp gets a 1-frame collision grace.** Any entity that crosses a screen boundary gets `DisabledTag` for exactly one frame (the system re-enables it the next iteration). A body arriving on the far side can never find itself inside another body at the start of that frame.

Respawn-placed asteroids are still unguarded — if you manually respawn into an occupied region they'll overlap, but the ooze behavior should handle it without getting stuck. If that's still an issue I can add spawn-position rejection.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 08:42 UTC

Good. The shrink code was entirely removed by the linter — the build passes but there's no shrinking at all. Now let me address your questions honestly before implementing anything.

---

## On the shrinking idea

**Yes, it makes sense mechanically — and your framing is the correct one.** The reason single cells teleport is geometrically exact: a cell's collision polygon was just a face of the parent's compound shape. When it detaches, its polygon is flush against the hole it left — the cell walls touch the asteroid walls with zero clearance on all sides. The collision system sees immediate deep overlap from every direction simultaneously, and the net correction vector is near-zero (opposing contacts cancel). The result is oscillation or a sudden lurch when one side briefly dominates.

A 10% contraction toward each cell's own centroid creates a uniform gap all around, so:
- The cell is no longer in contact with the asteroid walls at birth
- It can drift out or be pushed out by any net force without fighting opposing contacts
- It's physically invisible at game speed — nobody notices a 10% area difference in a crumbling asteroid

**The constraints you stated are exactly right:**
- Only single-cell fragments (multi-cell chunks have irregular geometry that doesn't perfectly fill their hole anyway)
- Only the detaching cell, not the surviving body
- Only at the moment it becomes its own entity

---

## Implementation plan

The linter keeps stripping struct fields I add to `FractureProcess`/`FractureTiming`. The robust approach: put the scale in **`FractureProperties`** as a material field — it semantically belongs there ("how much does this material's cells contract when they become dust-sized?") and material fields have never been stripped. Brittle glass might contract less (cells fly off with clean edges), ductile metal more (cells crumble inward). Add it to each preset.

Then: in `BuildFragments` and `SplitLive`, when `idxs.Count == 1` and it's not the continuer, apply `mat.DetachCellScale`. No extra parameters to thread, no fields on transient process structs.

**Plan in brief:**
1. Add `DetachCellScale` (default 0.90f) to `FractureProperties` + all four presets
2. In `BuildComponentSpec`: when `idxs.Count == 1` and `cellIsDetaching` (a bool parameter replacing the float), scale = `mat.DetachCellScale`, otherwise 1.0
3. The `cellIsDetaching` bool = `fling && idxs.Count == 1` (flung single-cell = detaching, not continuer)
4. No new struct fields anywhere

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 08:51 UTC

## On the shrinking idea — yes, it's the right fix, and here's why exactly

The root cause of the teleport is geometric: a single cell's collision polygon is **exactly flush** with the hole it left in the parent. From the very first frame it exists as its own entity, all of its faces are touching the asteroid's inner walls simultaneously. The collision system generates contacts pointing inward from every direction — they partially cancel each other out, the net correction is near-zero, and on the frame where one side momentarily dominates the cell gets a large uncancelled push and rockets out.

A 10% contraction toward the cell's own centroid creates uniform clearance on all sides. The cell is born with a small gap between itself and the surrounding walls, so contacts don't exist yet. It can drift out naturally — or at worst gets one clean outward push instead of a multi-directional mess.

**Why only single cells, not chunks:** a multi-cell fragment has an irregular, non-convex outline that doesn't mirror the hole it left. Its cells were already pulling apart from each other, so there's no "perfect overlap" situation. Only a single isolated cell has an outline that is an exact fit for its former location.

**Why only the detacher, not the surviving asteroid:** the surviving body's geometry genuinely fills the space it occupies. Shrinking it would create gaps in the asteroid surface.

---

## Implementation plan

**Where the scale lives:** `FractureProperties.DetachCellScale` — a float alongside the other material properties (`Toughness`, `Brittleness`, etc.). I've tried adding it to `FractureProcess`/`FractureTiming` structs twice and the linter keeps stripping it. Material fields have never been touched. It also makes physical sense there: brittleness and grain affect how cells break, this affects how they behave the instant they become independent.

**Where the scale is applied:** inside `BuildComponentSpec` in `FractureSimulator.cs`. That function already has `mat` (the material) available. The condition is simple: `fling == true && idxs.Count == 1`. When both are true, scale each vertex of that one cell toward the cell's own centroid by `mat.DetachCellScale`. The continuer (`fling=false`) and all multi-cell chunks skip it.

**The vertex math:** for each vertex `v` of the cell with centroid `c`: `scaled_v = c + (v - c) * scale`. This shrinks toward the centroid uniformly. Cell area scales by `scale²` — visually negligible at 0.90.

**Presets:** all four materials get `DetachCellScale = 0.90f` to start. You can tune per material later (glass might warrant more clearance since it shatters into many tiny cells more often than metal).

---

## Warp removal

Remove `WrapSystem` from the systems list and delete the class. Nothing else touches it. Without warp, fragments fly off-screen and disappear — which is fine and actually cleaner for a destruction demo. The TTL system already cleans up debris.

---

## Fullscreen / bigger window

The borderless + queried-size approach failed last session (window didn't appear). Safest path: just raise the `W, H` constants to match your monitor. What's your screen resolution? I'll set the constants directly — no SDL query, no borderless flag, just a larger windowed frame. If you want it to cover the full screen, the simplest reliable method on Linux/SDL is `SDL_SetWindowFullscreen` called *after* the window is created, which I can add as a single line in `SdlGameWindow` or a post-creation call in the demo.

What resolution should I target, and do you want fullscreen-proper or just a large window?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-05 09:12 UTC

<ide_opened_file>The user opened the file /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_opened_file>
What does the fling variable represent and why is it necessary for cells to shrink?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 09:12 UTC

`fling` controls whether a fragment gets an ejection impulse from `FragmentMotion` — the radial scatter added on top of the parent's inherited velocity. It's `true` for pieces genuinely flying off as separate entities, and `false` for the **continuer**: the largest surviving piece that keeps the propagating crack front and shouldn't get a random scatter on top of its existing motion.

So in `SplitLive`, the continuer gets `fling: false` (keep your velocity, keep cracking), everything else gets `fling: true` (you're separating, here's a fling impulse). In `BuildResult` at the end of a detach-mode fracture, the final remaining piece also gets `fling: false` (it already shed its chunks, no extra push).

Using `fling` to gate the shrink is **pragmatically correct but conceptually overloaded** — it was originally only about velocity. The conditions happen to align perfectly: any piece with `fling: false` is the surviving asteroid body, and you absolutely don't want to shrink its cells (that would create gaps in the asteroid surface and corrupt its collision shape). Any detaching single cell always has `fling: true`.

However, there's a subtle case worth knowing about: in finalize-at-end mode (`DetachOnSplit = false`), `BuildResult` is called with `fling: false` for the whole final burst. That means single cells in the finalize-at-end path won't shrink — which is actually fine there, since all cells eject simultaneously and there's no "hole still in the asteroid" for them to be stuck inside. The shrink only really matters in the detach mode where a cell comes off while the rest of the asteroid is still present and solid.

If you ever wanted a cleaner separation, the right approach would be an explicit `isDetachingFromLiveBody` bool alongside `fling`. For now the coupling is harmless since the two concerns are always in sync.

Can you do deep check on the collision system, particularly the case of internal sat of composed asteroids?
