This only happens when there's concave shapes and the fragments is inside a deep hole.
The problem must be that the normal uses the whole compound shape's centroid instead of the specific cell that collided.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 10:31 UTC

Let me read the actual current state of the collision code carefully before diagnosing:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 10:31 UTC

Found it. When both bodies are `CompoundShape` (which they always are — single-cell fragments get a single-part `CompoundShape` too), the code hits the **first branch** (`sa is CompoundShape`), not the second. It uses **A's cells** to orient normals, then flips everything. The "cell centroid" orientation is done relative to A's cells — meaning the **fragment's cells**, not the asteroid's. Then it flips.

So for a fragment cell inside an asteroid crater: the normal is oriented "fragment-cell → asteroid" (using fragment's cell as reference), then flipped to "asteroid → fragment-cell". That's B→A, pointing from asteroid *toward* fragment — which is the inward-pointing contact direction. Exactly backwards for a body inside a concave shape.

The fix: when **both** are compounds, we need two separate loops — each asteroid cell produces a contact oriented by **that asteroid cell** vs the fragment, and each fragment cell vs the asteroid is used separately. But actually the simpler correct model is: **always use the asteroid's cells** to orient, since the crater geometry lives there. Read:

When fragment is inside asteroid crater, we need normals from **asteroid cells** pointing **toward the fragment**. That means we want the `sb is CompoundShape` branch to run with `sb = asteroid`. But the spatial query makes the asteroid `entityB` when it's the larger, more numerous candidate — not guaranteed.

The real fix: **remove the asymmetry entirely.** When both are compounds, iterate both sides and take the most reliable orientation — or better, just let the **SAT itself** orient the normal correctly (it already does, using each pair's polygon centroids) and stop re-orienting afterward. The cell-centroid re-orientation in `CollectContacts` is the right idea but redundant when SAT already handles it, and the branch logic in `CollectPairContacts` is fighting it:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 10:31 UTC

Now I see the full picture. `CollectContacts` is called on the **asteroid** (`posA = asteroidPos`) and produces normals pointing "asteroid-cell → fragment" (by the `posB - cellWorld` check). That's correct. But `CollectPairContacts` only reaches `cb.CollectContacts` when `sa` is NOT a compound — but since all bodies are compound, it always hits the **first branch** (`sa is CompoundShape ca`), runs fragment's cells against the asteroid, then flips the whole lot. That undoes the correct orientation.

The fix: when **both** are compounds, call `CollectContacts` on the **larger / asteroid body** directly. The cleanest approach — handle the compound-vs-compound case explicitly:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 10:31 UTC

But there's a subtlety: which is A and which is B at query time depends on the spatial query order, not on which body is the asteroid. The fix above always uses B's cells — but either body could be the asteroid. The safe fix is to **always collect from both sides and deduplicate**, or better: **collect from whichever compound is larger** (more cells = more likely to be the asteroid with concave geometry). But the simplest robust approach is to collect from **both** compounds, since `CollectContacts` is cell-level and handles overlaps:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 10:32 UTC

Clean. The root cause: **every body in this engine is a `CompoundShape`** (even single-cell fragments, since `BuildShape` always returns one), so the `sa is CompoundShape` branch was always taken — which collected normals from the **fragment's** cell, then flipped them. For a fragment inside a crater that produces normals pointing inward (the fragment's own cell centroid is surrounded by the asteroid), so after the flip they pointed further inward — exactly the backwards push you were seeing.

The fix: in the compound-vs-compound case, use the **larger** compound's cells as the reference (the asteroid has many cells, a fragment has one). The larger body's cell normals point outward from its own geometry — for a crater wall that means pointing *toward the fragment*, which is the correct separation direction. For equal-size pairs (fragment vs fragment) it's symmetric and doesn't matter which side wins.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-05 12:51 UTC

Very good. Let me ask some questions about what the current engine/destruction system is capable of.
I'm wondering if this fracture system can support asteroid-on-asteroid collisions that fracture both the asteroids if the impact is high enough, while also push them as it does now. Would there be any problem with this?
Is it possible in this engine to make "piercing" bullet that doesn't vanish on the first impact but instead continues while losing velocity on each hit based on the impact, mass and toughness of the material hit, etc.?
Would it be possible to make a "slow motion"-style skill where everything in the game moves slower while maintaining the same fps?

If you have other cool ideas that are possible now using this engine tell me, these are all things I'm considering for the final game

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 12:53 UTC

## Asteroid-on-asteroid fracture

Fully supported by the current architecture with one extra piece of work. `CollisionEvent` already carries both entities, the contact normal, and contact point. You'd subscribe to it, check both have `FracturableBody`, compute relative velocity, and call `BeginFracture` on each (treating the other as the impactor). The reduced-mass energy formula already handles symmetric collisions.

The one real complication: `CollisionEvent` fires **every frame** two bodies are overlapping, not just on first contact. A slow-moving asteroid grazing another would trigger hundreds of fracture calls. You need a "first contact" gate — either a set of pairs seen last frame (new pair → fracture, ongoing pair → ignore), or the sub-threshold accumulator already in place (only fracture when energy exceeds toughness, which it won't if the impact velocity is low). The second approach is almost free and physically correct: a gentle nudge doesn't fracture, a hard collision does.

The recoil already works — the contact solver handles the push-apart impulse independently of fracture, so physics response and fracture are already decoupled.

## Piercing bullets

Straightforward engine addition, one missing primitive: `PhysicsQueries.RaycastAll` returning all hits along a segment sorted by distance. The current `Raycast` stops at the nearest. With `RaycastAll`, the bullet system iterates hits in order, reduces a `RemainingEnergy` (or `RemainingVelocity`) budget per hit — each hit calls `BeginFracture` with the current energy and subtracts the energy absorbed — and stops when the budget runs out or the bullet exits.

The energy-per-hit model: absorbed energy = min(bullet's remaining energy, what the material would absorb to fracture). If the material is too tough to fracture, the bullet loses that energy and stops. If it fractures, the bullet loses the fracture threshold energy and continues with the remainder. This maps directly onto the existing `ComputeBudget` return value — the `Absorbed` field tells you exactly how much energy went into the target.

No structural changes needed. Add `RaycastAll` to `PhysicsQueries`, add a `PiercingTag { float Energy; }` component, change the bullet handler from destroy-on-hit to energy-reduce-on-hit.

## Slow motion

Already fully supported, no engine changes. The game loop currently does:

```
int steps = fixedStep.Advance(frameTime);
for (int i = 0; i < steps; i++) session.Update(FixedDt);
```

A `timeScale` float (1.0 = normal, 0.2 = 5× slowdown) just scales `frameTime` before passing it to `fixedStep.Advance`. The physics advances fewer steps per real frame. Rendering runs at full FPS regardless. Particles need `_fx.Update(dt * timeScale)` to match. That's the whole implementation — one multiply. The multi-frame crack propagation slows down proportionally for free since it's step-counted, which would look spectacular.

---

## Other things this engine enables that you might not have considered

**Resonance / accumulation attacks.** The sub-threshold `AbsorbedEnergy` field already persists on the body. A rapid-fire low-energy weapon — a machinegun, a vibration beam — builds up damage invisibly until the body suddenly shatters. Glass (low toughness) would shatter after 2-3 hits; metal might absorb 20. This is already in the engine, it just needs a different weapon profile.

**Area detonation / shockwave.** A bomb that queries all bodies within a radius, then calls `BeginFracture` on each with a synthetic impact direction (body_pos - explosion_center) and energy falling off with distance squared. The spatial index already supports radius queries. Fragments from multiple asteroids would peel off simultaneously.

**Asteroid-on-asteroid chain fracture.** You already have co-propagating fronts. A fragment flying from asteroid A hits asteroid B — that's a normal bullet hit with the fragment's mass and velocity. The `FragmentSpec` already carries mass and linear velocity. The fragment then becomes a natural impactor and you get chain reactions for free, just by routing collision events through the fracture service.

**Drilling / shaped charge.** High directionality (≈1.0) + high blast + fast crack speed concentrates all energy in a narrow forward channel, punching a tunnel through the asteroid rather than shattering it radially. Already tunable today — try Directionality 0.95, Blast 0.05 in the sandbox.

**Crack arrest / freeze beam.** A weapon that sets `FramesPerIteration` to a very high value on any body it touches, locking cracks mid-spread. Combined with slow motion this could let you freeze a shattering asteroid in place mid-explosion — a showpiece mechanic.

The thing I want to see most is Asteroid-on-Asteroid chain fracture with a force threshold for triggering fractures. In this demo you need to start checking if the asteroid overlaps with something on spawn and spawn them so they don't spawn inside each other. For the impact hit direction it would make a lot sense to take into account the rotation of the asteroids so it influences the impact direction.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 13:19 UTC

Clean. Here's what landed:

## Spawn overlap check
`FindSpawnPosition` tries up to 40 random positions, rejecting any that overlap an already-placed asteroid this wave (radius + radius + 20px margin) or are within 150px of the player. Falls back to a player-safe random if all 40 attempts fail. Radius is `AstRadius.Value` uniformly.

## Asteroid-on-asteroid chain fracture

**Approach gate:** only fires when relative velocity at the contact point (including both bodies' rotational velocity: `v_linear + ω × r`) is above `AsteroidCollisionThreshold` (80 px/s, tunable constant). After the contact solver applies its impulse the bodies are separating, so the condition naturally won't fire again next frame.

**Pair tracking:** `_activeCollisionFractures` (HashSet of `(minId, maxId)`) prevents the same contact from triggering twice. It's cleared in `OnFractureCompleted` and `OnFractureSplit` when a body is destroyed.

**Rotation-influenced impact direction:** the crack propagation direction for each body is `normalize(vRel_contact)` — the full relative velocity at the contact point — so a spinning asteroid that grazes another will crack differently from a head-on hit.

**Velocity trick:** to get `BeginFracture`'s `(impactorVelocity - bodyLinear)` formula to produce the correct contact-point relative velocity, we pass `vRel + vA.Linear` for body A and `-vRel + vB.Linear` for body B. The subtraction of `bodyLinear` inside cancels perfectly, leaving `vRel` for both energy calculations.

**WeaponProfile for impacts:** `MomentumTransfer = 0` (the contact solver already handles momentum exchange — no double-counting), `BlastFraction = 0` (impacts don't vaporize cells, they crack them), reduced `ImpactSpin`.

Even with `SurfaceEfficiency = 0.12`, the crack budget overwhelms all bonds on every single hit. The fix is an `EnergyScale` on `WeaponProfile` that deflates the raw kinetic energy before the threshold check, giving proportional fracture behavior across a wide speed range.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 14:10 UTC

Clean. Here's the full picture:

## Why it always shattered before

Two 53,000-mass asteroids at 200 px/s produce ~1 billion units of raw kinetic energy. Even after `SurfaceEfficiency = 0.12` takes its 88% cut, the crack budget is still ~500× a full bullet hit. Every bond in the body breaks. The threshold check never had a chance.

## The fix: `WeaponProfile.EnergyScale`

A multiplier applied to `eImpact` **before** the toughness threshold. The same 53,000-mass asteroid pair at 200 px/s with `EnergyScale = 0.0008` delivers ~38,000 energy units — roughly half a bullet hit. At 80 px/s (threshold speed) it's ~6,000 — a light chip. The **quadratic** v² relationship is preserved, so fast collisions are proportionally much more destructive than slow ones.

**Why 0.0008?** Typical asteroid reduced-mass ≈ 26,500; typical bullet effective mass ≈ 0.2. Ratio = 132,500. To equalize a 200 px/s asteroid collision with a typical 900 px/s bullet: `(200/900)² / 132,500 ≈ 0.0004`. I set the default at 0.0008 to make even moderate-speed impacts do visible damage. Tune live with **Ast E scale** in the FRACTURE panel — step size is 0.00005 for fine control.

## Blast

**Ast blast** defaults to 0.08 (8% vaporisation crater): cells within the top 8% of absorbed energy at the contact point vaporize to dust. A grazing hit at the threshold will chip one or two surface cells; a direct head-on collision will open a small crater where they met.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-05 14:25 UTC

Why doesn't our energy model work properly with hits between asteroids?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 14:26 UTC

It's a fundamental calibration asymmetry in the model, not a bug. Let me explain all three layers:

## 1. The reduced-mass mismatch

The model computes `eImpact = 0.5 · mRed · vRel²`. For a bullet hitting an asteroid:

```
mBullet ≈ 0.2       mAsteroid ≈ 53,000
mRed = (0.2 × 53,000) / 53,200 ≈ 0.2        → eImpact ≈ bullet's own KE
```

`mRed` collapses to the bullet mass because of the huge mass ratio. The whole system — toughness, bond strengths, SurfaceEfficiency — was calibrated around this.

For two equal asteroids:

```
mA ≈ mB ≈ 53,000
mRed = 53,000 / 2 = 26,500       → eImpact is 130,000× larger at the same speed
```

The reduced mass is orders of magnitude heavier, so even at 80 px/s you get ~85 million energy units vs ~81,000 for a bullet. The fracture parameters never saw numbers like that.

## 2. The threshold doesn't save you

The toughness threshold `= Toughness × struckCellMass` does grow with asteroid size, so a larger asteroid requires more energy to fracture. But the threshold only removes a *fixed additive amount*, while the surplus grows as `mRed × v²`. At the minimum trigger speed (80 px/s), the surplus is already enough to break every bond many times over.

For a bullet near threshold, the surplus is small and you get a proportional chip. For equal-mass asteroids, you're never "near threshold" — even the slowest triggerable hit produces a budget that dwarfs the total bond strength of the body.

## 3. Point-deposition vs. distributed contact

Bullets deposit all their energy at one cell because they stop immediately. Asteroid-on-asteroid contact is distributed across an extended interface, but the model still seeds a single crack front at one cell. The full energy gets funnelled into that one point, which amplifies the already-huge budget locally.

## Why EnergyScale is the right tool, not a hack

In real material fracture, the fraction of bulk kinetic energy that converts to fracture surface energy is very small for large collisions between equal-mass bodies — most goes into elastic deformation, acoustic waves, and heat. The ratio is roughly `G_c / (0.5 · mRed · v²)` where `G_c` is the fracture toughness — which is exactly what `SurfaceEfficiency × (1 − kFrac)` models, but calibrated for bullet-scale energies. `EnergyScale` is restating that coupling coefficient at a scale appropriate for large collisions. It's physically justified: a rock dropped from 1m onto another rock chips it; the same rock dropped from a crane shatters it — the *fraction* of KE going into fracture is roughly the same, but the absolute energy is different.

## The clean long-term fix

If you want asteroid-on-asteroid to work without a magic number, the right model is **contact-area stress**: `eEffective ∝ σ² / (2E_material)` where σ is the peak stress at the contact zone. This gives `eEffective ∝ v^(5/3) / R^(1/3)` (Hertz contact theory), which naturally scales with asteroid size and speed without needing calibration between bullet and asteroid mass regimes. It's a ~10-line change to `BeginFracture` for the asteroid-on-asteroid path. Worth doing when you build the game proper and want the physics to be self-consistent, but the `EnergyScale` approximation is perfectly adequate for the sandbox.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-05 14:30 UTC

Let's discuss this long-term fix. Explain how it would work and why it would adapt to both similar-mass impacts and bullet-asteroid.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 14:32 UTC

## Why the current model breaks for equal masses

The current formula is `eImpact = 0.5 × mRed × v²`. This treats fracture energy as directly proportional to kinetic energy, which holds when one body is much lighter than the other. For a bullet hitting an asteroid, `mRed ≈ m_bullet` because the mass ratio collapses the formula down to the bullet's own KE — the asteroid is so massive it barely moves, so nearly all the kinetic energy of the *bullet* concentrates at the impact point.

For two equal asteroids, `mRed = m/2`, which is 130,000× heavier than a bullet. The formula gives 130,000× more energy, but the fracture system has no mechanism to compensate. Both bodies' material is being hit by the equivalent of 130,000 simultaneous bullets, so every bond breaks instantly. The issue isn't the total energy in the collision — it's that the model assumes **all kinetic energy couples directly into fracture**, which is only true for a point impactor that stops immediately.

## What Hertz actually says

In reality, fracture is driven by **stress intensity at the contact zone**, not by total kinetic energy. When two large bodies collide, the contact spreads over an area — and as force increases, so does the contact area, which dilutes the stress. The peak stress scales as:

```
σ ∝ (E*)^(2/15) × mRed^(1/5) × v^(2/5) × R*^(-3/5)
```

where `E*` is the effective elastic modulus and `R* = R₁R₂/(R₁+R₂)` is the reduced radius. The critical exponents:

- **v²→v^(4/5)**: doubling speed gives 1.74× more damage, not 4×.
- **mRed¹→mRed^(2/5)**: doubling mass gives 1.32× more damage, not 2×.
- **R*^(-6/5)**: a smaller contact radius concentrates stress — a 3px bullet tip creates far higher stress per unit KE than two 110px asteroids touching.

The effective fracture energy scales as `σ² × contact_volume ∝ (mRed × v²)^(4/5) / R*^(1/5)`. The exponent is 4/5, not 1 — that's the whole fix.

## Why this self-corrects for both cases

For a **bullet hitting an asteroid**:
- `mRed ≈ 0.2`, `v = 900` → `(mRed × v²) = 162,000`
- `R* ≈ R_bullet ≈ 3` px (bullet radius ≈ 0, so `R*` collapses to bullet's radius — very small)
- `(162,000)^(0.8) / 3^(0.2) ≈ 16,000 / 1.25 ≈ 12,800`

For **two equal asteroids** at 200 px/s:
- `mRed = 26,500`, `v = 200` → `(mRed × v²) = 1.06 × 10⁹`
- `R* = 55` px (both asteroids, `R*= R/2`)
- `(1.06×10⁹)^(0.8) / 55^(0.2) ≈ 15,000,000 / 2.22 ≈ 6,800,000`

Ratio: `6,800,000 / 12,800 ≈ 530×` — versus the current model's `6,500×` ratio. That's still bigger, but now the `R*` radius term does real work: the large contact area of two asteroids touching suppresses stress by `2.22 / 1.25 ≈ 1.8×` relative to a tiny bullet contact, and crucially this ratio would grow larger for bigger asteroids, keeping the model self-consistent as you scale bodies up. More importantly, at 80 px/s (threshold approach speed):

- `(26,500 × 6,400)^(0.8) / 55^(0.2) ≈ 2,600,000` vs at 200 px/s: `6,800,000`

That's a `2.6×` difference in fracture energy between a gentle graze and a hard hit — the system now responds proportionally to speed, which is exactly what was missing.

## What would change in the code

One function, one change — the `eImpact` line in `BeginFracture`:

```csharp
// Current
float eImpact = 0.5f * mRed * vRelN * vRelN * energyScale;

// Hertz
float R1 = ImpactorRadius(world, impactor);    // from Collider AABB half-diagonal
float R2 = BodyRadius(body);
float Reff = (R1 * R2) / (R1 + R2);           // reduced radius
float E_mat = mat.Toughness * 200f;            // proxy for elastic modulus (calibrate once)
float ke = mRed * vRelN * vRelN;
float eImpact = Calibration * MathF.Pow(ke, 0.8f) / MathF.Pow(E_mat * Reff, 0.2f);
```

`Calibration` is a single constant tuned so a default bullet still hits with the same energy as today — the bullet case degrades gracefully since `R*` → bullet radius makes the denominator small and partially compensates. For bullets you'd still get a `WeaponProfile.EnergyScale` as an authoring override for different bullet types. For asteroid-on-asteroid, `EnergyScale` would default to 1 and the physics handles it.

The `ImpactorRadius` for bullets would return the visual bullet circle radius (3px). For asteroid-on-asteroid it would return the rough bounding radius from the `CompoundShape`'s local AABB diagonal. Neither needs to be exact — the `R*^(1/5)` exponent is so shallow that a 2× error in radius only changes the result by `2^0.2 ≈ 1.15×`.

## Verdict

Worth doing before the game ships, not right now. The model you'd end up with has no `EnergyScale` magic number anywhere — bullet hits, asteroid glances, and high-speed collisions all fall out of the same formula with their physical behavior intact. It would also mean that giant asteroids (when you add them) automatically require harder hits to fracture without any re-tuning. The change is about 20 lines and touches only `BeginFracture` plus a radius-extraction helper.

Now it feels very good, just as I wanted. The only problem is with the impact between the surviving asteroid and its own detached fragments: if the asteroid is spinning really fast the impact generates enough force to make it spin in the opposite direction; feels very wrong. How could we fix it? No coding for now.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-05 15:55 UTC

The root cause is a **spurious contact impulse** between a fragment and the asteroid it just came from. Let me walk through exactly why it happens.

## What's physically happening

When a fragment detaches, `FragmentMotion` gives it `bodyLinear + ω×r + ejectSpeed`. That velocity is correct — it's what the surface of the spinning asteroid was already doing at that point, plus a small radial kick outward. At the instant of birth the fragment is *at the surface* of the parent, barely separated.

The problem: on the very next fixed step, the collision system sees these two bodies overlapping or in contact. It computes a contact normal and asks: *are they approaching or separating?* Because of the spin, the parent's surface near the fragment is moving tangentially, not radially. The relative velocity at the contact point has a large tangential component. Depending on how the contact normal ends up oriented (and concave shapes make this unreliable), the solver may see the tangential velocity as an "approach" along the contact axis — it applies an impulse to prevent that "approach", and the tangential component of that impulse changes the asteroid's angular momentum. If the normal is slightly mis-oriented, the impulse runs in the wrong direction.

The positional correction (`TrySeparate`) compounds this: it nudges both bodies apart along the contact normal, shifting the fragment to a slightly different position. From that new position, the velocity solver recomputes relative motion and may see a different approach angle. Two frames of this can flip the spin.

## Why the existing ghost system doesn't fix it

`FractureGhost` disables ALL asteroid-layer collisions for the fragment. The user set `Remaining = 0.0f`, so it's permanently off. Even if re-enabled, it's a blunt instrument — it also prevents the fragment from hitting a different asteroid or the player during the grace window, which may feel wrong.

## The fix options, ranked

**Option A — Re-enable FractureGhost with a short duration (~0.15s)**. Existing mechanism, zero new code, but affects all collisions not just parent collisions. Simplest.

**Option B — Parent immunity: skip collision response between a fragment and its specific birth parent for a short window**. More targeted. Attach a `SpawnedFrom { Entity Parent; }` component to freshly detached fragments; the collision system skips velocity solving (and optionally positional correction) for that specific pair. Other asteroids and the player still collide with the fragment normally. The component is removed after ~10 frames or when either entity is destroyed. This is the most correct fix.

**Option C — Skip velocity solving when the contact depth is shallow for new fragments**. A variant of the existing `DeepNoBounce` logic applied to fragment-asteroid pairs in their first few frames. Lets positional separation work (they ooze apart) without the impulse that flips spin.

**Option D — Increase eject speed to guarantee separation by frame 2**. Doesn't fix the mechanics, just makes the overlap less likely. Fragile.

## My recommendation

**Option B** is the right one. The invariant you want is: a fragment cannot impart an impulse on the asteroid it was just part of, because that contact is an artifact of the instantaneous geometry of spawning, not a real physical collision. Targeting it by parent identity rather than blanket ghosting keeps the fragment physically active to the rest of the world from birth. Implementation is maybe 20 lines across a new component, one attachment in `SpawnBody`, and a few lines in `CollisionSystem`. Want me to build it?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-06 14:33 UTC

<ide_opened_file>The user opened the file /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/info/meta_notes.md in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_opened_file>
Would it make sense to assign a component with some kind of mask and a 5/10 frame ttl to all resulting entities and check that their mask is different when triggering the fracturing. This way we wouldn't need to keep a reference to the parent. On split/detach this component can also be inherited so there's no problem if the parent gets split the very next frame.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 14:35 UTC

Yes, this is cleaner than parent tracking. Let me confirm the design and surface the few details worth locking before coding.

## Why it's better

No entity reference means no dangling pointer problem when the parent is destroyed mid-TTL. The group ID is a pure integer comparison — `O(1)` and trivially serializable if you ever need replay. And the inheritance through re-splits is exactly right: all pieces from a fracture chain are siblings of the original body, so they should all be immune to each other regardless of which generation they came from.

## The one detail to lock: what gets the component

Not just the detaching fragments — **the surviving/continuing body too**. That's the entity the fragments are in contact with at the moment of birth. If you only attach it to fragments, the surviving asteroid has no group ID and the check `A.Id == B.Id` can never be true for a fragment-vs-parent contact.

So the rule: when any fracture event fires (`FractureCompletedEvent`, `FractureSplitEvent`), generate one group ID and attach it to **every entity produced**, including the continuer that still has a `FractureProcess`.

## Inheritance on re-split

When the continuing body (which already carries group ID 5 with 7 frames left) splits again, the new pieces should inherit **ID 5**, not get a fresh ID 6. This keeps first-generation and second-generation siblings in the same group. The TTL resets to the full duration on inheritance so late-born pieces get the same grace window.

The check in `SpawnBody` / the fracture handlers: does the originating entity already have a `FractureGroup`? If yes, reuse its ID and reset `FramesLeft`. If no (asteroid that was never fractured before), mint a new ID.

## Whether to skip positional correction too

The spin reversal comes from the **velocity impulse**, not from `TrySeparate`. Positional correction just nudges positions apart gently (capped at 8 px/frame), which can't directly add angular momentum — it shifts positions but the angular impulse comes only from `SolveContact`. So you could allow `TrySeparate` while skipping `GatherContact` and still solve the problem cleanly.

The counterargument: the position shift from `TrySeparate` means the fragment is at a slightly different world position for the next velocity solve, which can create small artificial relative velocities. Given that the TTL is only 5–10 frames and fragments are actively moving outward anyway, I'd skip **both** for same-group pairs. No positional correction needed — ejection velocity already separates them, and any brief visual overlap at spawn is invisible at game speed.

## The TTL value

5 frames at 120 Hz = ~42ms. That's enough for a fragment moving at 200 px/s to travel 8px from its spawn point — well clear of the asteroid surface. 10 frames is more conservative and still imperceptible. 8 frames as a default seems right.

## Asteroid-on-asteroid case

Two colliding asteroids each start their own fracture process independently, so each gets a fresh group ID. Their resulting fragments are in different groups and collide normally with each other — which is what you want, since those fragments are genuinely from different rocks.

Ready to build it on your say-so.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-06 14:55 UTC

What if the parent's ttl also refreshes?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 14:56 UTC

The parent (`ev.Body`) is destroyed in both handlers, so its TTL is discarded by definition — it ceases to exist. What you're really asking about is the **other first-gen siblings** still flying around with their original TTLs counting down when a second split fires.

In the current code they're unaffected. When a second split happens at frame 4, A1 and A3 (first-gen siblings with TTL=4 remaining) don't get refreshed — only the newly spawned second-gen pieces get a fresh 8. Since the group ID check is symmetric, A1 and A3 lose their immunity to the second-gen pieces 4 frames after the second split, not 8.

**Is this worth fixing?** Probably not. By the time the continuer splits a second time (which requires several frames of crack propagation), the first-gen fragments have been in flight for at least those same frames and are already well clear of the impact zone. Their immunity expiring slightly earlier than the second-gen pieces' is inconsequential.

The case where it would matter is a pathologically fast re-fracture — e.g., a second bullet hit on the continuer one frame after the first split, before first-gen fragments have moved anywhere. In that case refreshing all entities with the same group ID would help. But this is the same scenario that the `_activeCollisionFractures` gate already partially handles.

If you want to add it, it's a `ForEach<FractureGroup>` filtering by group ID and resetting `FramesLeft = 8` — about 5 lines. I'd leave it unless you actually observe the problem in play.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 14:58 UTC

`RefreshFractureGroup` does a single `ForEach<FractureGroup>` filtering by ID and resets `FramesLeft = 8` on every existing sibling before the new pieces spawn. Both handlers call it right after computing `fg`, so any first-gen fragment that's still near the surface when a second split fires gets its window fully renewed.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 15:13 UTC

## Honest feedback on each

---

### #0 — Spin-influenced directionality for asteroid collisions

The spin influence on **impact direction** is actually already there: `impactDir = normalize(vRel)` where `vRel = vcB - vcA` already incorporates both bodies' `ω × r`, so a grazing spin-dominated hit already tilts the crack direction tangentially. What's missing is a **separate directionality tunable** for asteroid collisions (currently it reuses the bullet slider) and a **blend control** between "crack follows the contact normal" (pure head-on, geometry-driven) and "crack follows the full relative velocity" (spin+linear blend). Something like: `impactDir = normalize(lerp(-contact.Normal, vRel, spinBlend))`. Small change, one new param. Clean and worth doing.

---

### #1 — Cell sub-fracture from absorbed energy

The concept is right — currently a cell either survives intact, cracks off as a solid fragment, or vaporizes. The middle zone (significant absorbed energy, below blast threshold) producing internal sub-structure is physically motivated and would make the fracture feel deeper.

**The architecture fits cleanly**: the per-cell `energy[]` array is already tracked through propagation. You'd add a `SubFractureThresh` to `FractureProperties` (e.g., 0.3× blast threshold), and cells in the middle zone get tessellated into sub-cells at finalization. The sub-body gets `BeginFracture` called immediately with the absorbed energy and original direction — the whole existing pipeline handles it.

**Two genuine risks:**
1. **Recursion**: a sub-cell absorbs energy → sub-sub-fractures → infinite subdivision. Needs a `MinFragmentArea` hard stop and ideally a depth limit.
2. **VoronoiTessellator on arbitrary convex polygon**: currently it generates cells inside a circle. Clipping seeds to an irregular convex polygon boundary needs verification or a small adaptation. This is the main unkn own.

I'd design the thresholds carefully before this one. **Medium complexity.**

---

### #2 — Polygon debris from pulverized cells

This is purely visual — the physics doesn't change at all, cells still pulverize the same way. The `CellPulverizedEvent` already carries world position, area, and parent velocity. You just replace (or augment) the particle dust with actual polygon entities.

The cut algorithm can be simple: N random straight cuts through the centroid producing convex sectors. For a convex polygon with N cuts from centroid you get N convex wedge-shaped pieces trivially — no polygon clipping needed, just split by angles.

**Design questions worth locking before coding:**
- Do polygon debris **replace** particles entirely or coexist? (I'd say: polygon debris for cells above some area threshold, pure particles for small cells — below MinFragmentArea)
- How many cuts per cell? 2–4 depending on area.
- What spin do they get? Some random angular + inherited parent spin, fading over TTL.

**Cleanest of the three.** No engine changes, just a new component, renderer addition, and the cut math. Performance is fine since they're collider-free and short-lived.

---

## Recommended order and scope

**Do #0 first** — trivial, one param, done in minutes. 

**Do #2 second** — purely additive, no regression risk, makes the sandbox feel finished before tackling harder work.

**Do #1 last** — deepest change, benefits from knowing the tessellator behavior and having debris ready (since sub-fragmented cells produce their own debris). Also worth deciding: does #1 need to work in multi-frame mode (crack reaches a cell → it immediately starts sub-fracturing while the main front continues), or only at finalization? Multi-frame is much harder. Finalization-only is manageable and covers 95% of visible cases.

One honest concern about #1: it pushes the entity count up fast. A 15-cell asteroid where 5 cells sub-fracture into 3 pieces each gives you 15+ new collidable bodies per hit. If asteroids spawn more asteroids spawn more asteroids, the scene can get congested quickly. The `MinFragmentArea` gate is load-bearing here — sub-cells below that area should go straight to polygon debris (#2), not spawn physics bodies.

Want to go ahead in this order?

## #0 — Spin-aware asteroid crack direction

New **`Ast dir spin`** tunable (FRACTURE group, default 1.0). The crack direction into each body is now `lerp(contactNormal, relativeVelocityDir, AstDirSpin)`:

- **0** = pure contact normal — head-on crack, geometry only, ignores spin.
- **1** = full relative velocity at the contact point (which already carries both bodies' `ω × r`) — current behavior preserved as the default.

The negation symmetry holds: body B's direction is exactly `−impactDirAB`, since both the normal and relative velocity flip sign for the other body. I also corrected the inverted fallback while there (it was dead code, but now consistent).

## #2 — Polygon debris

**Engine:** `CellPulverizedEvent` now carries the cell's world-space polygon, its inherited surface velocity (`linear + ω×r`), and parent spin — computed once at the vaporization site.

**Demo:** `SpawnCellDebris` cuts the cell polygon with 1 or 2 random lines through its centroid (`SplitConvexByLine`, a half-plane clip that's guaranteed to keep each piece convex) → 2 or 4 chunks. Each becomes a collider-less entity with `Transform + Velocity + TimeToLive + DebrisPiece`, inheriting the cell's motion plus an outward scatter and a randomized spin around the parent's. They're driven by the existing `MovementSystem` / `TimeToLiveSystem` / `PreviousStateSystem` — no new system needed.

**Render:** a new loop fills each debris polygon (convex, so `FillPolygon`) faded by `Remaining / MaxTtl`, drawn under the dust haze so chunks read as solid and dust floats over them. Dust and polygon debris coexist exactly as you wanted.

Tunables: **`Debris ttl`** (0.8s) and **`Debris scatter`** (40 px/s) in a new DEBRIS panel group. The window is 1080px tall now so the extra rows fit fine.

## The logger — `Engine/Diagnostics/ForceLog.cs`
A zero-overhead-when-off static sink. Each instrumented site is guarded by `ForceLog.On(category, entityId)` so the formula string is only built when that channel is live. Lines read `[f<frame>] <CAT> e<id> result = term + term + …`.

- **Press `L`** in the demo to toggle → writes to `forces.log` (auto-flushed, in the run dir).
- `ForceLog.Categories` is a `[Flags]` filter. **Default excludes `Integration` + `Drag`** (the per-body-per-step firehose) but includes every discrete event. Set to `All` or any subset.
- `ForceLog.EntityFilter` = an entity id to isolate one body.

## Instrumented sites (13 channels)
Every place velocity/spin changes, engine and demo:

| Cat | Where | What's logged |
|---|---|---|
| `Integration` | PhysicsSystem | `v += F/m·dt`, `ω += τ/I·dt` |
| `Drag` | PhysicsSystem | exponential `v *= e^(-drag·dt)` |
| `Thrust` | ApplyForce | applied force → Δv |
| `Contact` | CollisionSystem | net normal+friction impulse per contact, ΔvA/ΔωA/ΔvB/ΔωB |
| `Separation` | TrySeparate | Baumgarte positional push |
| `Energy` | BeginFracture / OnCollision | reduced-mass impact energy, budget split, collision approach |
| `Recoil` | BeginFracture | impact kick on the struck body |
| `Fling` | FragmentMotion / continuer | fragment linear (parent + ω×r + eject) & angular (shear + random) breakdown |
| `Debris` | SpawnCellDebris | polygon-chunk vel & spin |
| `Spawn` | demo | initial asteroid/bullet vel & spin |

The fracture path sets `ForceLog.CurrentBody` so fragment-construction lines (which run before the new entities exist) attribute to the source body.

## The doc — `info/forces.md`
Full reference: every force with its **formula**, the **intuition** behind it, and the **tunable** that controls it, organized to match the log categories — plus a "force flow per frame" section showing the system order, and logger usage notes. It cross-references the Hertz long-term energy fix you parked in `meta_notes`.

One thing to note for your analysis: `Contact` logs the *converged net* impulse per contact (once), not all 6 solver iterations — that's the number you actually want for tuning, without 6× noise.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 19:01 UTC

I'll insert a concepts/glossary section between the logger usage and section 1:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-06 19:01 UTC

Added a **§0 Concepts & terms** section right before the force reference. It covers exactly the distinctions you asked about:

- **Linear vs. angular as two parallel worlds** — a side-by-side table mapping each linear quantity (position, velocity, mass, force, impulse) to its rotational twin (rotation, ω, inertia, torque, angular impulse), with the key note that in 2-D rotation is a single scalar while linear motion is a 2-D vector.

- **Force vs. impulse vs. velocity** — the three things that get conflated, spelled out: velocity is the *state*, force is a *continuous push* that changes velocity over time (`Δv = F/m·dt`), impulse is an *instantaneous* velocity change (`Δv = P/m`). Plus the engine's rule of thumb: engines/drag → forces, impacts → impulses.

- **Geometry terms** — normal, tangent, contact point, lever arm, penetration depth, each with the intuition (e.g. why a long lever arm produces more spin).

- **The 2-D cross product** — explained as a scalar with its two roles: torque from an off-centre force (`r × F`), and `ω × r` as "the linear velocity a spinning body has at a point", which is the term that makes fragments inherit spin.

- **Other terms** — reduced mass, restitution, friction, symplectic Euler.

The rest of the doc already used these terms consistently, so the sections below now have a glossary to lean on. Pure documentation change — no code touched.

2. Key Technical Concepts:
   - Cell/bond-graph fracture model: asteroids pre-fractured into convex Voronoi cells joined by a bond graph; fracture-energy budget breaks bonds; connected components = fragments; pulverized cells = vaporized.
   - Multi-frame crack propagation: stateful `CrackFront` (per-cell energy field, frontier, budget) advanced by `FractureCrackSystem`; co-propagating fronts share `Broken[]`; finalize-at-end v1.
   - Mid-spread detachment: `SplitLive` partitions a split body into fresh re-centred pieces; continuer keeps cracking (carries remapped sub-process), others fling.
   - ECS (World, Entity{Id,Version}, ISystem, ForEach, GetComponent ref, HasComponent, AddComponent immediate, DestroyEntity deferred, RemoveComponent deferred, QueryEntities returns List).
   - Reduced-mass impact energy `eImpact = ½·mRed·vRelN²·EnergyScale`; threshold = Toughness·struckCellMass; surface/kinetic split.
   - Contact solver: sequential impulses (6 iterations), Coulomb friction, restitution bias, Baumgarte positional correction (capped at MaxCorrection=8px, DeepNoBounce=6px).
   - CompoundShape SAT collision with cell-oriented normals (critical for concave craters); all bodies are CompoundShape even single cells.
   - Fixed timestep 1/120s + render interpolation (PreviousStateSystem).
   - FractureGroup: 8-frame sibling immunity for positional correction (velocity solving unaffected).
   - ForceLog diagnostics: zero-overhead-when-off static sink with `[Flags]` category filter.
   - Linter behavior: sometimes strips newly-added fields from structs (notably `FractureProcess`/`FractureTiming`), so durable fields went onto `FractureProperties`.

4. Errors and fixes:
   - **Blast inverted**: threshold was `blast*EnergyTotal` (lower blast = more vaporization). Fixed to `(1-blast)*EnergyTotal` strict `>`. User: "even at blast = 0.05 cells hardly ever separate and instead every cell impacted gets obliterated."

User diagnosed: "The problem must be that the normal uses the whole compound shape's centroid instead of the specific cell that collided." Fixed by compound-vs-compound branch using larger compound's cells.
   - **Asteroids always full-shatter on collision**: huge reduced-mass energy (~10⁹). Added `WeaponProfile.EnergyScale` (0.0002), threshold 20px/s.
   - **DetachCellScale always 0**: User diagnosed "DetachScale is always = 0, that's why the shrink flag is always false." Root: `SpawnAsteroid` built FractureProperties manually omitting new fields (also CrackSpeed); Tuner had no sliders. Fixed both.
   - **Shrink applied to chunks/never**: First gated only on `idxs.Count==1` at split-time but single cells rarely appear at split (they finalize). Fixed by also applying in BuildResult/BuildFragments; then user said "should only be single cells, not chunks" and "only when a single cell becomes an individual asteroid and only on that cell, not the remaining surviving asteroid" → condition `fling && idxs.Count==1`.

ForceLog.C

This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_selection>
How do I set the categories in ForceLog

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 19:38 UTC

Set `ForceLog.Categories` to any bitwise combination of `ForceCat` values. The field is a public static so you can write to it anywhere — usually right after the `ToggleForceLog` call or at startup.

**Examples:**

```csharp
// Only destruction events (energy + recoil + fling) — typical for tuning fracture feel
ForceLog.Categories = ForceCat.Energy | ForceCat.Recoil | ForceCat.Fling;

// Everything except the per-step firehose (the built-in default)
ForceLog.Categories = ForceCat.Default;  // = All & ~Integration & ~Drag

// Everything, including per-step integration and drag
ForceLog.Categories = ForceCat.All;

// Turn off a single category from the current set
ForceLog.Categories &= ~ForceCat.Contact;

// Add a category back in
ForceLog.Categories |= ForceCat.Integration;
```

**Optional: narrow to one entity**

```csharp
ForceLog.EntityFilter = someEntityId;   // only lines for this entity
ForceLog.EntityFilter = -1;             // back to all entities
```

The categories available are: `Integration`, `Drag`, `Thrust`, `Contact`, `Separation`, `Recoil`, `Fling`, `Debris`, `Spawn`, `Energy`.

Does the formula that applies torque to colliding bodies take into account mass? If so, how is it possible that a single detached cell can completely change the spin direction of its parent when colliding?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 20:37 UTC

Let me read the collision system to check the exact formulas.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 20:38 UTC

Yes, the formula **does** correctly account for mass and inertia. Looking at [CollisionSystem.cs:318](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L318):

```csharp
vA.Angular += c.InvIA * Cross(c.rA, p);
```

And the impulse magnitude is already reduced by the asteroid's inertia through the `NormalMass` computation at line 276:

```csharp
float kn = invMassA + invMassB + invIA * rnA * rnA + invIB * rnB * rnB;
```

So `p = NormalMass × closing_speed` — a heavy, hard-to-spin asteroid produces a smaller impulse AND applies it through `1/IA`. Both sides are correct.

---

**The real cause is the FractureGroup interaction.**

`TrySeparate` skips positional correction for siblings ([line 165-167](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L165)). But `GatherContact` has no such check — velocity impulses run normally. The consequence:

1. Without FractureGroup: the Baumgarte correction pushes the cell away in 1–2 frames, the contact disappears, impulse runs once or twice.
2. **With FractureGroup**: no positional push → the cell stays overlapping with the parent for all 8 immunity frames → the contact is re-detected and the velocity solver fires every single frame → ~8× more cumulative angular impulse.

The FractureGroup was meant to prevent teleportation, but it accidentally prolongs contact duration, letting impulse accumulate far longer than a natural collision would.

**The tradeoff:** if you suppress velocity impulses for FractureGroup pairs too (in `GatherContact`), the cell passes through the parent silently — which you previously didn't want. The middle ground would be to let velocity impulses run but also let positional correction run at reduced strength (say half), so the cell actually drifts away and the contact is short-lived. Want to try that?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-07 20:41 UTC

The FractureGroup component was actually introduced to solve this very issue (which it didn't), so it certainly isn't the culprit.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 20:44 UTC

You're right to push back. Let me look at this more carefully rather than blaming the attempted fix.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 20:44 UTC

The formula is correct — both impulse magnitude and angular application are properly mass/inertia weighted. The actual reasons a small cell can still reverse a large parent's spin:

---

**1. Compound manifold multiplies contacts**

`CollectPairContacts` generates one `Contact` object per overlapping *cell pair*. A cell embedded in a crater may overlap 3–5 parent cells simultaneously → 3–5 independent contacts in `_contacts`, each independently applying `Δω += InvIA × Cross(rA, p)` to the parent. These are summed, not averaged. The parent receives 3–5× the angular impulse a single contact would produce.

**2. Lever arm at the rim**

`Δω = Cross(rA, P) / I`. For a contact at the asteroid's rim, `rA ≈ R`. Inertia scales as `M·R²`, so:

```
Δω ≈ R · P / (M·R²) = P / (M·R)
```

P is cell-mass-bounded (≈ m_cell × v_rel), so:

```
Δω ≈ m_cell · v_rel / (M · R)
```

For a fast-spinning asteroid ejecting a surface cell: `v_rel` includes restitution amplification `(1+e)` and the fling speed. At `R = 50px`, `m_cell/M = 0.1`, `v_rel = 300 px/s`: `Δω ≈ 0.6 rad/s` per contact — times 5 contacts = 3 rad/s from a single frame.

**3. Restitution at high closing velocity**

The bias term `−e·vn0` effectively targets velocity `(1+e)·v_approach` instead of zero, multiplying the impulse by up to 2× for elastic contacts. This compounds point 1 and 2.

---

The multiple-contacts issue is likely the dominant one. The contact manifold isn't merging contacts — it's summing their independent angular contributions onto the same body. Would you want to look at constraining manifold contacts (e.g. keeping only the deepest contact per pair, or averaging the angular contribution across contacts)?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-07 20:47 UTC

Can some contact logs help your analysis? If so, what should I look I for?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 20:47 UTC

Yes, a focused log can answer two specific questions. Here's exactly what to capture and what to look for:

**Setup: narrow the log to just the moment it happens**
```csharp
// In Program.cs, before spawning the asteroid you want to watch:
ForceLog.EntityFilter = <asteroid_entity_id>;  // narrow to one body
ForceLog.Categories = ForceCat.Contact;         // only contact lines
```
Then trigger one collision and stop logging immediately (press L twice). The log will be short enough to read manually.

---

**Question 1 — are there multiple contacts between the same pair in one frame?**

Look for consecutive lines with the same `[f NNNNN]` and the same two entity IDs, e.g.:
```
[f  13552] Contact e34 vs e347: ...  ΔωA 1.2
[f  13552] Contact e34 vs e347: ...  ΔωA 1.8
[f  13552] Contact e34 vs e347: ...  ΔωA 2.1
```
If you see this, the manifold is generating multiple independent contacts per pair per frame, and their `ΔωA` values are additive — that's the multiplier I suspect.

---

**Question 2 — what is the total ΔωA for the asteroid in that frame?**

Sum all `ΔωA` (and all `ΔωB` where the asteroid appears as B) for the same frame number. Compare that sum to the asteroid's actual spin before and after the frame. If the sum matches the observed spin change, the contacts fully explain it. If the sum is much smaller than the observed spin change, something else is happening (positional correction, a force elsewhere).

---

**What I'm expecting to find:** several Contact lines with the same pair in the same frame, with `ΔωA` values that individually look small but sum to a spin reversal. If instead there's only one contact per pair but `ΔωA` is already huge on its own, then the lever-arm + restitution amplification is the dominant path and the manifold isn't the issue.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 21:02 UTC

This data is very revealing. Let me read it carefully before drawing conclusions.

The first thing that jumps out: `e491` spawns at frame 23312, and on frame 23313 — its very first frame of existence — it has contacts with **~60 distinct entities** simultaneously (e317, e94, e70, e141, e89, e57... all the way to e17). This isn't fragment-vs-parent physics. **e491 materialized inside an occupied field of existing bodies.**

From that context, here's what the log confirms and denies:

---

**Multiple contacts per pair: confirmed and severe**

e375 has **12 contacts** with e491 in one frame. e499 has 6, e405 has 6, e641 has 5, e587 has 4. The manifold over-generation is real.

**However, for bodies with symmetric penetration, they partially cancel:**
- e622: ΔωA = +37.971 then −41.269 → net −3.3 rad/s
- e77: ΔωA = +11.166 then −12.918 → net −1.75 rad/s

So multi-contact for a deeply-embedded body generates big opposing impulses that mostly wash out. For a body contacted on only one side (typical fragment-grazing-parent), they stack instead of canceling.

---

**Mass accounting is working correctly**

e491's mass from the log: `ΔvB / P ≈ 22.42 / 3,822,300 → invMassB ≈ 5.9e-6 → mass ≈ 170,000`. Its ΔωB per contact is 0.001–0.02 rad/s. Across all 60 contacts, total spin change for e491 is roughly **0.05–0.1 rad/s** against a spawn spin of −1.65. The heavy body is essentially unaffected. This is correct physics.

The small fragments (mass ~500–1000) on the A side get ΔωA of ±20–40 rad/s per contact because their InvIA is large — again correct.

---

**What this does NOT show**

This isn't the fragment-vs-parent reversal case. To see that, you'd want a log where a detached single cell (mass ~500) hits a partially-destroyed parent (mass ~2000–5000, i.e., 4–10 cells remaining). In that regime `m_cell / M_parent` is no longer negligible and the physics genuinely allows spin reversal.

---

**The question this raises**

Is the spin reversal you're observing actually happening to a *freshly-spawned* asteroid that materializes overlapping with existing fragments, rather than during a proper fragment-vs-parent collision? The spawn for e491 produced 60 simultaneous contacts with large opposing ΔωA values on the surrounding bodies — that would look exactly like sudden chaotic spin changes in the scene.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-07 21:27 UTC

<ide_opened_file>The user opened the file /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/Program.cs in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_opened_file>
I get it now, the contact works fine, the problem must be in the fracturing algorithm / formula. Even hits with the bullet seem to give some unnatural "reverse" spin to the asteroid, and when I set the fracture speed to a very low number I noticed that the sudden change in spin is only applied at the end of the whole fracture event (not on every detach). This happens even when I set "impact spin" to 0, so the problem must be somewhere else. Also it seems to affect particularly the largest surviving part in a fracture.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 21:29 UTC

That's a much tighter clue. Let me read the simulator — the spin assignment to the continuer is the prime suspect now.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 21:30 UTC

Found it. [FractureSimulator.cs:546](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs#L546):

```csharp
return (linear, -angular);
```

`FragmentMotion` computes `angular = parentAngular + ImpactSpin*shear + spinRand` and then **returns its negation**. Every flung fragment gets the opposite spin of the parent. With `ImpactSpin=0` and `spinRand≈0`, that's `-parentAngular` — exact spin reversal. The log line on 545 even records `-angular` as the final value, which is the return value.

The continuer goes through the `else` branch at line 358–359 (no `FragmentMotion` call, direct `angular = input.BodyAngular`), which is why it's unaffected in `DetachOnSplit=true` mode. But in `DetachOnSplit=false` (finalise-at-end), **all** fragments including the largest go through `FragmentMotion` and get negated spin — that's the reversal you see at the end of the whole fracture event.

The fix is one character:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-07 21:30 UTC

Build clean. The `-angular` was the entire root cause — all flung fragments (and the largest piece in finalise-at-end mode) were getting their spin reversed relative to the parent on every fracture.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-08 11:00 UTC

[info/game_design.md](info/game_design.md) is written. Here's what went in and a few things I'd flag for your reaction:

**On the boundary:** I went with the exponential repulsion field as the primary suggestion — it's one `ForEach` over all `Velocity` components each frame and feels organic. I also noted the "ring of invisible anchor masses" as an alternative if you want something more physical. The key advantage of the force approach is that asteroids and aliens feel it too, so the field naturally clears itself over time.

**On the ship health:** I suggested no HP bar — damage is purely structural and visible (cells crack, bonds weaken, cells detach). This fits the engine perfectly and is more readable than a number. The ship "dies" when its core cell is destroyed. Worth discussing if you want something more traditional.

**Four open questions at the bottom** that would meaningfully affect implementation:
1. Mouse-aim or keyboard rotation? (Changes control feel significantly)
2. Slow-mo: does it scale world physics time, or is it a visual effect only? (World time = the impressive version, affects alien bullets too)
3. Does the ship remain fractureable after damage, or respawn clean?
4. Anomaly triggers: random timer, scripted per wave, or player-triggered?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-08 19:29 UTC

Honest take, point by point:

---

**16× world size** — I'd push back here. 7680×4320 with a hard entity cap means the world will often feel empty between waves, which fights the "chaos" vibe directly. The density of action is what makes the physics destruction readable and exciting. I'd suggest **8×** (two 1920×1080 tiles in each dimension) as a starting point and scaling up if it still feels cramped after playtesting. You can always make the world bigger; pulling it back once content is tuned is harder.

**Anchor-ring boundary alternative** — Instead of a force field, place N invisible static RigidBody "anchor" masses arranged in a ring just outside the world boundary. Each one exerts a gravity-style attraction: `F = G·mAnchor·mObj / d²` toward the anchor. For a complete ring, the net pull on anything near the center sums to near-zero (forces cancel). As an object drifts toward one side, the near anchors dominate and it gets pulled back. The emergent behavior is a soft elastic boundary with no hard direction — objects curve back rather than getting pushed. It's heavier to compute than a field check (`N × entities` force applications per frame) but feels more organic, and since these are just invisible force sources with no collision, the cost is manageable.

**Functional ship cells** — The strongest design idea here, but the repair system is the load-bearing piece that could make or break it. Without a solid repair loop, losing a cannon cell in wave 2 means the rest of the run is crippled and unfun. My suggestion: **mid-run cell salvage**. When an alien is destroyed, it might drop one of its own cells as a drifting pickup. The player flies over it to attach it to their hull. Attaching an alien cell to a weapon slot could give a slightly different weapon variant (alien cannon fires blue homing shots, for example). This makes combat rewarding beyond score, gives a repair path, and creates interesting decisions about whether to risk going for a pickup in a debris field.

**Shotgun** — Agreed on fast pellets, and I'd make them raycast rather than physical projectiles. At short range, all rays hit the same debris cluster. At range, the spread cone means only 1-2 rays connect. This is computationally cheap (N raycasts per shot vs N entities), and the "clear multiple debris cells" use case is served naturally without adding to entity count.

**Piercing round** — The momentum-based penetration model is elegant and exactly the right call for this engine. Model it as a physical projectile with very high mass. The reduced-mass energy transfer `eImpact = ½·mRed·vRelN²` already handles it: against a light asteroid `mRed ≈ mAsteroid`, so the bullet barely slows. Against a massive boulder `mRed ≈ mBullet`, so the bullet absorbs most of the reaction and stops. No special-casing needed, just tune mass and starting velocity.

**Slow-mo** — Your version (true time-scale, movement boost) is the right call. One thing to nail: "finer control" should mean reduced angular drag + increased turn torque for the ship during slow-mo, so the ship responds to steering inputs at near-normal speed even though the world is at 0.3×. Not a speed boost, a precision boost. The player can thread gaps and aim at specific cells that they couldn't normally hit.

**Wave system** — Here's a concrete spec to react to:

- Each wave has a **spawn budget** (increases each wave: e.g. wave N budget = `base + N * scaling`).
- Budget costs per variant: Gravel = 1, Standard = 3, Spinner = 4, Boulder = 8, Armored = 10, Unstable = 5.
- A **bias vector** shifts toward expensive/aggressive variants as waves progress (early waves: mostly Standard/Gravel; late waves: mostly Boulder/Armored/Spinner with Gravel filler).
- Spawn all at once at wave start; if entity cap is hit mid-spawn, defer remaining spawns until existing entities fall below the cap. This also creates a design incentive: clear debris fast to allow the next wave to fully spawn.
- Between waves: ~5 second gap (repair/upgrade window — see below).

**Drone AI** — For the obstacle avoidance I'd use **context steering**: at each AI step, cast N rays in a circle; weight each direction by (pull toward player) minus (push away from nearby objects). The ship steers toward the highest-weighted direction. It's simple, ECS-friendly, and handles asteroid fields naturally without pathfinding. I can spec this in more detail when we get there.

**Progression — the real problem** — You're right that score alone isn't enough. My honest suggestion: **give the run a shape**. Rather than infinite survival, the run has a win condition: survive **N waves** and destroy the **mothership** that spawns on wave N. This gives the player a clear goal, a narrative arc ("I got to wave 7 this time, wave 9 the time before"), and a meaningful ending. The leaderboard can show both "best wave reached" and "cleared" status. Combined with the functional cell system and cell salvage, the progression within a run becomes: manage your ship's degradation, make smart weapon choices, survive long enough to face the mothership.

---

Want me to revise [game_design.md](info/game_design.md) with all of this baked in, then we can start planning the actual implementation order?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-09 13:56 UTC

You're completely right about the anchor-ring — I described it backwards. A ring of attractive masses would pull things toward the border, which is the opposite of the goal. The correct version is a ring of **repulsive** masses: objects near the border get pushed away from the nearest repulsive anchor back toward the center; objects in the center have all the pushes cancel. Functionally identical to the exponential field but more physical-feeling. Both work — the exponential field is simpler to implement, the repulsive ring is easier to intuit visually (you can even render faint "warning zones" around each repulsor). I'll note both in the doc.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-09 13:57 UTC

Doc updated. A few things I want to flag honestly:

**The HUD question at the bottom is actually load-bearing.** The functional cell system only works as a mechanic if the player knows in real-time which cells they've lost. A small ship diagram in the corner (lit = intact, dark = gone) is probably not optional — without it the player has to look at their ship mid-chaos to assess their situation, which is frustrating. Worth deciding early because it affects how you author the ship structure data.

**The piercing round "lateral impulse clamp" needs a decision.** Two clean options: (a) apply full contact physics but the bullet's mass is so high it barely deflects anyway — pure physics, no special casing; (b) explicitly suppress velocity changes on the normal-to-direction axis in the contact solver for piercing-tagged entities — less physical but gives a more satisfying "drilling" feel. Option (a) is simpler to implement and might be enough. Worth testing before adding special logic.

**Endless mode after the mothership** needs a brief thought before implementation: since the run doesn't end, the score needs to keep meaning something. The kill-chain multiplier stacking up to ×4 should probably continue climbing in endless mode (e.g. cap raises to ×8 or removes entirely), otherwise the post-boss section feels flat score-wise.

Ready to start planning the implementation order whenever you are.

Let me chronologically analyze this conversation, which is a continuation from a previous session that was summarized.

## Starting Context (from summary)
The conversation began mid-session with context from a previous conversation about building a 2D game engine in C# specialized in physics-based destruction for "Asteroids on Steroids." The engine was complete with:
- ECS architecture
- Cell/bond-graph fracture model
- Multi-frame crack propagation
- CompoundShape SAT collision
- Fixed timestep 1/120s + render interpolation
- ForceLog diagnostics system
- FractureGroup component

## Session Flow

### 1. ForceLog Categories Question
User asked how to set categories in ForceLog (had selected `ForceLog.C` in IDE). Assistant read ForceLog.cs and explained the `ForceCat` enum and how to use bitwise combinations to filter categories. Also explained `EntityFilter`.

Key info from ForceLog.cs:
```csharp
[Flags]
public enum ForceCat
{
    None=0, Integration=1<<0, Drag=1<<1, Thrust=1<<2, Contact=1<<3,
    Separation=1<<4, Recoil=1<<5, Fling=1<<6, Debris=1<<7, Spawn=1<<8, Energy=1<<9,
    All=~0,
    Default = All & ~Integration & ~Drag,
}
public static class ForceLog {
    public static bool Enabled;
    public static ForceCat Categories = ForceCat.Default;
    public static int EntityFilter = -1;
    public static long Frame;
    public static Action<string> Sink = Console.WriteLine;
    public static int CurrentBody = -1;
    public static bool On(ForceCat cat, int entity = -1) => ...;
    public static void Write(ForceCat cat, int entity, string detail) => ...;
    public static string V(Vector2 v) => ...;
}
```

### 2. Spin Reversal Analysis Question
User asked: "Does the formula that applies torque to colliding bodies take into account mass? If so, how is it possible that a single detached cell can completely change the spin direction of its parent when colliding?"

Assistant analyzed CollisionSystem.cs and explained:
- Formula IS correct: `vA.Angular += c.InvIA * Cross(c.rA, p)`
- Hypothesized three causes: compound manifold multiplies contacts, lever arm effect, restitution amplification
- Suggested the main cause was multiple contacts per pair per frame from compound manifold generating multiple ContactInfo objects

### 3. User Correction on FractureGroup
User said: "The FractureGroup component was actually introduced to solve this very issue (which it didn't), so it certainly isn't the culprit."

Assistant revised analysis and identified:
1. Multiple manifold contacts per pair (confirmed hypothesis)
2. Lever arm amplification: `Δω ≈ r × m_cell × v_rel / I_parent`
3. Restitution multiplier (1+e)

### 4. Contact Log Analysis Request
User asked what to look for in contact logs. Assistant gave specific instructions:
- Set EntityFilter to narrow to one body
- Look for multiple Contact lines with same entity pair in same frame
- Sum ΔωA across all contacts for an entity in one frame

### 5. Contact Log Data Shared
User shared a large log section showing e491 spawning at frame 23312 and immediately having ~60 contacts with different entities at frame 23313. Assistant analyzed and correctly identified this as a **spawn overlap problem** - e491 materialized inside an occupied field. Key finding: e491 (large asteroid, ~170k mass) barely changes ΔωB per contact (~0.001 rad/s each). The small entities on the A side get large ΔωA values.

### 6. User Narrows Down the Spin Reversal Bug
User provided crucial clues:
1. Spin reversal happens at END of whole fracture event, not on every detach
2. Setting ImpactSpin=0 doesn't fix it
3. Affects particularly the largest surviving part
4. Even bullet hits cause it

This points to `FractureSimulator.FragmentMotion` or `BuildResult`.

### 7. Bug Found and Fixed
Assistant read FractureSimulator.cs and found the bug at line 546:
```csharp
return (linear, -angular);
```

`FragmentMotion` computes `angular = parentAngular + ImpactSpin*shear + spinRand` and returns its **negation**. Every flung fragment gets the opposite spin of the parent. With ImpactSpin=0, they spin at `-parentAngular` — exact reversal.

The log line (545) also confirms: `$"... = {-angular:0.##}"` — it logs the negative.

The continuer (fling=false path) correctly uses `angular = input.BodyAngular` without negation. But in DetachOnSplit=false (finalise-at-end) mode, ALL fragments including the largest go through FragmentMotion and get negated spin.

**Fix applied:**
```csharp
// Line 545-546: changed from:
$"... = {-angular:0.##}");
return (linear, -angular);
// to:
$"... = {angular:0.##}");
return (linear, angular);
```

Build succeeded (0 errors, 0 warnings).

User said: "We can start designing the game now, the engine is complete and works just as I envisioned it."

1. World map size: 4X normal window (independent of window size), no warp

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-10 10:32 UTC

Good plan. Here's my take on the architecture and order.

## Config Architecture

**One shared `GameConfig` class library** (`GameEngine/GameConfig/GameConfig.csproj`) holding only data classes + a JSON loader. No game logic, no engine dependency beyond the types it already exports (`WeaponProfile`, `FractureProperties`).

```
GameEngine/
  Engine/               ← unchanged
  Platform/Sdl/         ← unchanged
  GameConfig/           ← NEW: data classes + JSON loader only
    GameConfig.csproj
    game_config.json    ← THE canonical file
    PlayerConfig.cs
    AsteroidVariantConfig.cs
    WaveDefinition.cs
    AlienConfig.cs
    SkillsConfig.cs
    ScoringConfig.cs
    WorldConfig.cs
  Game/                 ← NEW: game logic, loads config read-only
    AsteroidsGame.csproj
    ...
  Demos/AsteroidDemo/   ← existing tuning demo, gains a Save button
```

Both `AsteroidDemo` and `Game` reference `GameConfig`. The demo's Tuner loads from `game_config.json` at startup and gets a **Save** button that writes it back. The game loads it read-only. Tuning loop: run demo → tweak sliders → Save → game picks up changes automatically.

`game_config.json` structure:
```json
{
  "World": { "Width": 3840, "Height": 2160, "BoundaryDepth": 200, ... },
  "Player": { "ThrustForce": 800, "MaxSpeed": 400, "RotationSpeed": 5.0, ... },
  "Weapons": { "Cannon": {...}, "Shotgun": {...}, "Piercing": {...}, "Grenade": {...} },
  "Materials": { "Rock": {...}, "Ice": {...}, "Metal": {...} },
  "AsteroidVariants": { "Standard": {...}, "Boulder": {...}, ... },
  "Waves": [ { "Number": 1, "Theme": "Tutorial", "Budget": 20, ... }, ... ],
  "Aliens": { "Drone": {...}, "Bruiser": {...} },
  "Skills": { "Dash": {...}, "Turbo": {...}, "SlowMo": {...} },
  "Scoring": { "CellAreaMultiplier": 1.0, "ChainWindow": 3.0, ... }
}
```

---

## Implementation Order

**Phase 1 — Config foundation** *(unblocks everything)*
1. `GameConfig` project, data classes, JSON loader
2. Migrate current demo Tuner values into `game_config.json`; demo loads from it + adds Save button

**Phase 2 — Playable core** *(get something you can actually fly)*
3. Game project skeleton: game states (Playing, Paused, GameOver, Menu stub), main loop
4. World bounds + camera (player follow + cursor bias)
5. Player ship prefab (named cells with roles, mouse-aim, thrust/strafe)
6. HUD — ship cell mini-diagram (this is not optional, do it here not at the end)
7. Cannon wired into game (already calibrated in demo)

**Phase 3 — Game loop closes** *(run has a start, middle, end)*
8. Standard + Gravel asteroid prefabs from config
9. Collision damage → bond weakening → fracture (game-side wiring of existing engine)
10. Wave system: director, budget allocator, timer + area-threshold trigger, entity cap
11. Scoring system + kill-chain multiplier
12. Death condition (cockpit detached/pulverized → GameOver state)
13. Game over screen + local leaderboard (JSON file, top 10)
14. Main menu

**Phase 4 — Content expansion** *(tune each piece before adding the next)*
15. Remaining asteroid variants in order of wave introduction: Spinner → Boulder → Armored → Unstable
16. Skills: Dash, Turbo, Slow-mo (slow-mo needs a `TimeScale` multiplier in the fixed-step loop — small engine addition)
17. Remaining weapons: Shotgun, then Grenade, then Piercing
18. Aliens: Drone (context steering) → Bruiser (pursuit)
19. Cell pickup / repair mechanic

**Phase 5 — Endgame + polish**
20. Mothership (named cell groups, turret spawning, win state)
21. Endless mode scaling
22. VFX pass, sound stubs, settings screen

---

The critical path is **1 → 5 → 6 → 9 → 10 → 12**: config, ship, HUD, fracture wiring, waves, death. Everything in Phase 4–5 can be added incrementally without breaking the loop. The slow-mo `TimeScale` is the only engine extension needed — worth flagging now so we don't reach Phase 4 and find it requires more restructuring than expected.

Want to start with Phase 1 — the `GameConfig` project and migrating the demo?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-10 11:18 UTC

Can I use some external tool, or implement some custom UI to build presets (player and alien ships, mothership, maybe some asteroids)?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-10 11:19 UTC

Yes, and you already have the right medium for it.

**Recommendation: extend the existing browser prototype into a shape editor.**

You already have `prototypes/fracture-cells-v3.html` with Voronoi cell rendering, bond visualization, and live physics. Adding an authoring mode to it is much less work than building a new tool, and it exports JSON that `GameConfig` loads directly.

The editor would need:
- Click to place seed points (cells), drag to move
- Right-click a seed → assign role (cockpit / propeller / cannon / bumper / shield / turret / generic)
- Draw or import the outline polygon (or just a bounding convex hull)
- Live Voronoi preview as you place seeds
- Per-cell toughness multiplier override
- Export button → writes the preset JSON

Output format the game loads:
```json
{
  "name": "PlayerShip",
  "material": "metal",
  "outline": [[0,40],[25,-20],[-25,-20]],
  "cells": [
    { "seed": [0, 10], "role": "cockpit" },
    { "seed": [-20, -5], "role": "propeller", "toughnessMultiplier": 0.8 },
    { "seed": [20, -5], "role": "propeller", "toughnessMultiplier": 0.8 },
    { "seed": [0, -15], "role": "bumper", "toughnessMultiplier": 2.0 }
  ]
}
```

The game's prefab factory loads this, runs `VoronoiTessellator` on the seeds + outline, attaches cell roles as component data, and builds the `CompoundShape`. The browser editor never needs to know about the C# engine.

**Why not alternatives:**

- **Tiled / LDtk**: designed for tile grids, awkward for organic compound bodies
- **ImGui.NET in-engine editor**: good for tuning but higher setup cost; seeing the actual physics is nice but not necessary for authoring shapes
- **Hand-authored JSON**: fine for the player ship (6–8 seeds), but the mothership at 40–60 cells with named regions (core, shields, turret groups) genuinely needs a visual tool

**Scope split:**
- Player ship + drone + bruiser: could hand-author the seeds in JSON (small enough), but the browser editor makes iteration much faster
- Mothership: the browser editor is basically required — you need to see the named regions laid out spatially

The editor is probably a day or two of JS work given the prototype foundation you already have. Worth doing before implementing the game prefabs so you're not stuck hardcoding seed coordinates.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-10 13:13 UTC

Done. Open [prototypes/shape-editor.html](prototypes/shape-editor.html) in a browser.

**What's in it:**

- **Outline tab** — click to place polygon vertices, click near the first point (or double-click) to close. Drag vertices afterward to reshape. Four built-in presets: Player Ship, Drone, Bruiser, Mothership.
- **Seeds tab** — click inside the outline to drop a seed; drag to move; right-click to delete. Click an existing seed to select it, then choose its role from the 8-button picker (Generic / Cockpit / Propeller / Cannon / Bumper / Shield / Turret / Core). Each role has a distinct color. Auto-fill button populates seeds via the same grid+jitter as v3.
- **Fracture tab** — same propagation engine as v3 with all sliders; tests your shape directly.
- **Export / Save / Import / Load File** — Export writes centered JSON to the textarea (centroid shifted to origin); Save downloads it as `<name>.json`; Load File reads it back.

**JSON format the game will consume:**
```json
{
  "name": "PlayerShip",
  "material": "metal",
  "outline": [[0,-90],[28,-52],...],
  "seeds": [
    {"x":0,"y":-54,"role":"cockpit"},
    {"x":-30,"y":-22,"role":"cannon"},
    ...
  ]
}
```

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-10 13:34 UTC

Three features added:

**Grid** — Grid size selector (Off / 10 / 20 / 25 / 50 px) in the Outline tab, always visible on the canvas. Snap checkbox next to it. When snap is on, a blue ghost dot follows the snapped cursor position while placing vertices or seeds so you can see exactly where the point will land. Snap also applies during drag.

**Ctrl+Z** — Full undo stack (80 levels). Saves a snapshot before every mutation: vertex add/close, vertex drag start, seed add, seed drag start, seed delete, role change, auto-fill, mirror operations, clear, load preset, import. Double-click to close and load preset both save correctly.

**Symmetry** — Three buttons in the Seeds tab: None / ↔ Vertical / ↕ Horizontal. The purple dashed axis line is drawn on the canvas when active. Two operations:
- **Live mirror on placement**: when symmetry is active and you click to place a seed, a mirrored copy is automatically added on the other side (unless you click on the axis itself, within 3px).
- **Mirror Seeds →← button**: takes all seeds on the right side (vertical) or top side (horizontal), removes any on the opposite side, and generates mirrored copies — clean one-shot operation.
- **Mirror & Close ↔ button** (Outline tab): mirrors all current outline vertices horizontally and closes the polygon — draw just the right half, click this to complete it.
